<li>0.4% <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Japan" title="Chinese people in Japan">Chinese</a></li>
<li>0.6% other</li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>

<table class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" data-file-height="1147" data-file-width="512" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Nihongo.svg/18px-Nihongo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Nihongo.svg/27px-Nihongo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Nihongo.svg/35px-Nihongo.svg.png 2x" width="18"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;">You may need <a href="/wiki/Help:Installing_Japanese_character_sets" title="Help:Installing Japanese character sets">rendering support</a> to display the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> text in this article correctly.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Japan</b> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">日本</span> <i>Nippon</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">[ɲippoɴ]</a></span> or <i>Nihon</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">[ɲihoɴ]</a></span>; formally <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja">日本国</span></span> <i><span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Ja-nippon_nihonkoku.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Ja-nippon_nihonkoku.ogg" title="Ja-nippon nihonkoku.ogg">Nippon-koku</a></span></span></i> or <i>Nihon-koku</i>, meaning "State of Japan") is a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign</a> <a href="/wiki/Island_country" title="Island country">island nation</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>. Located in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, it lies off the eastern coast of the Asian mainland and stretches from the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">Sea of Okhotsk</a> in the north to the <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> in the southwest.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> that make up <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Japan" title="Names of Japan">Japan's name</a> mean "sun origin". 日 can be read as <i>ni</i> and means "sun", while 本 can be read as <i>hon</i> or <i>pon</i> and means "origin". Japan is often referred to by the famous epithet "Land of the Rising Sun" in reference to its Japanese name. Japan is a <a href="/wiki/Stratovolcano" title="Stratovolcano">stratovolcanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> consisting of about <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Japan" title="List of islands of Japan">6,852 islands</a>. The four largest are <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a>, which make up about ninety-seven percent of Japan's land area and often are referred to as home islands. The country is divided into 47 <a href="/wiki/Prefectures_of_Japan" title="Prefectures of Japan">prefectures</a> in eight <a href="/wiki/List_of_regions_of_Japan" title="List of regions of Japan">regions</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a> being the northernmost prefecture and <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Prefecture" title="Okinawa Prefecture">Okinawa</a> being the southernmost one. The population of 127 million is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population" title="List of countries by population">world's eleventh largest</a>. <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">Japanese people</a> make up 98.5% of Japan's total population. Approximately 9.1 million people live in the city of <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Capital_of_Japan" title="Capital of Japan">capital of Japan</a>.</p>
<p>Archaeological research indicates that Japan was inhabited as early as the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a> period. The first written mention of Japan is in <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">Chinese history</a> texts from the 1st century AD. Influence from other regions, mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_China" title="Imperial China">China</a>, followed by periods of isolation, particularly from Western Europe, has characterized <a href="/wiki/History_of_Japan" title="History of Japan">Japan's history</a>.</p>
<p>From the 12th century until 1868, Japan was ruled by successive feudal military <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shogun" title="Shogun">shoguns</a> who ruled in the name of the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor</a>. Japan entered into a long <a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">period of isolation</a> in the early 17th century, which was ended in 1853 when a United States fleet <a href="/wiki/Bakumatsu" title="Bakumatsu">pressured Japan</a> to open to the West. After nearly two decades of internal conflict and insurrection, the Imperial Court <a href="/wiki/Meiji_oligarchy" title="Meiji oligarchy">regained</a> its political power in 1868 through the help of several clans from <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB_Domain" title="Chōshū Domain">Chōshū</a> and <a href="/wiki/Satsuma_Domain" title="Satsuma Domain">Satsuma</a>—and the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> was established. In the late 19th and early 20th centuries, victories in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> allowed Japan to expand its empire during a period of increasing <a href="/wiki/Japanese_militarism" title="Japanese militarism">militarism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a> of 1937 expanded into part of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in 1941, which came to an <a href="/wiki/End_of_World_War_II_in_Asia" title="End of World War II in Asia">end in 1945</a> following the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">Japanese surrender</a>. Since adopting its revised <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">constitution</a> on May 3, 1947 during the <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">occupation</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Commander_for_the_Allied_Powers" title="Supreme Commander for the Allied Powers">SCAP</a>, Japan has maintained a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_state" title="Unitary state">unitary</a> <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_system" title="Parliamentary system">parliamentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> with an <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor</a> and an elected legislature called the <a href="/wiki/National_Diet" title="National Diet">National Diet</a>.</p>
<p>Japan is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Seven" title="Group of Seven">G7</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Group_of_Eight" title="Group of Eight">G8</a> and the <a href="/wiki/G20" title="G20">G20</a>—and is considered a <a href="/wiki/Great_power" title="Great power">great power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balance_of_Power_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balance_of_Power-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joshua_Baron_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joshua_Baron-19">[19]</a></sup> The country has the world's third-largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal)">economy by nominal GDP</a> and the world's fourth-largest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP)">economy by purchasing power parity</a>. It is also the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_exports" title="List of countries by exports">fourth-largest exporter</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_imports" title="List of countries by imports">fourth-largest importer</a>.</p>
<p>The country benefits from a highly skilled workforce and is among the most highly educated countries in the world, with one of the highest percentages of its citizens holding a tertiary education degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-20">[20]</a></sup> Although Japan has officially <a href="/wiki/Article_9_of_the_Japanese_Constitution" title="Article 9 of the Japanese Constitution">renounced its right to declare war</a>, it maintains a <a href="/wiki/Japan_Self-Defense_Forces" title="Japan Self-Defense Forces">modern military</a> with the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures" title="List of countries by military expenditures">eighth-largest military budget</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> used for <a href="/wiki/Self-defense" title="Self-defense">self-defense</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peacekeeping" title="Peacekeeping">peacekeeping</a> roles. Japan is a <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed country</a> with a very high standard of living and <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a>. Its population enjoys the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_life_expectancy" title="List of countries by life expectancy">highest life expectancy</a> and the third lowest <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a> rate in the world. Japan is well-known internationally for its major contributions to science and modern-day technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Table_A.17_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Table_A.17-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Japan" title="Names of Japan">Names of Japan</a></div>
<p>The Japanese word for Japan is <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja">日本</span></span>, which is pronounced <i>Nihon</i> or <i>Nippon</i> and literally means "the origin of the sun". The character <i>nichi</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">日</span>)</span> means "sun" or "day"; <i>hon</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">本</span>)</span> means "base" or "origin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The compound therefore means "origin of the sun" and is the source of the popular Western <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithet</a> "Land of the Rising Sun".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The earliest record of the name <i>Nihon</i> appears in the Chinese historical records of the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i>. At the end of the seventh century, a delegation from Japan requested that <i>Nihon</i> be used as the name of their country. This name may have its origin in a letter sent in 607 and recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Sui" title="Book of Sui">official history</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a>. <a href="/wiki/Prince_Sh%C5%8Dtoku" title="Prince Shōtoku">Prince Shōtoku</a>, the Regent of Japan, sent a mission to China with a letter in which he called himself "the Emperor of the Land where the Sun rises" (日出處天子). The message said: "Here I the emperor of the country where the sun rises send a letter to the emperor of the country where the sun sets. How are you".</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2066" data-file-width="4142" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg/220px-King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg/330px-King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg/440px-King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:King_of_Na_gold_seal_faces.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The "<a href="/wiki/King_of_Na_gold_seal" title="King of Na gold seal">King of Na gold seal</a>", said to have been granted to <a href="/wiki/Nakoku" title="Nakoku">Na</a> king of <a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">Wa (Japan)</a> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Guangwu_of_Han" title="Emperor Guangwu of Han">Emperor Guangwu of Han</a> in 57 CE. The seal reads "漢委奴國王". <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prior to the adoption of <i>Nihon</i>, other terms such as <i>Yamato</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">大和</span>, or "Great Wa")</span> and <i><a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">Wakoku</a></i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">倭国</span>)</span> were used. The term <i>Wa</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">和</span>)</span> is a homophone of <i>Wo</i> 倭 (pronounced "Wa" by the Japanese), which has been used by the Chinese as a designation for the Japanese as early as the third century <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> However, the Japanese disliked some connotation of <a href="/wiki/Wa_(Japan)" title="Wa (Japan)">Wa</a> 倭 (which has been associated in China with concepts like "dwarf" or "pygmy"), and it was therefore replaced with the substitute character <i>Wa</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">和</span>)</span>, meaning "togetherness, harmony".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fuzita_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-fuzita-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The English word Japan possibly derives from the historical <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> pronunciation of 日本. The <a href="/wiki/Old_Mandarin" title="Old Mandarin">Old Mandarin</a> or possibly early <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu Chinese</a> pronunciation of Japan was recorded by <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> as <i>Cipangu</i>. In modern <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, a Wu dialect, the pronunciation of characters <span class="t_nihongo_kanji"><span lang="ja">日本</span></span> Japan is <i>Zeppen</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">[zəʔpən]</a></span>. The old <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a> word for Japan, <i>Japun</i> or <i>Japang</i>, was borrowed from a southern coastal Chinese dialect, probably <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Fukienese</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ningbo_dialect" title="Ningbo dialect">Ningpo</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>—and this Malay word was encountered by <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> traders in South East Asia in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahd_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-ahd-30">[30]</a></sup> These <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">Early Portuguese traders</a> then brought the word to <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The first record of this name in English is in a book published in 1577 and spelled <i>Giapan</i>, in a translation of a 1565 letter written by a Portuguese Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Lu%C3%ADs_Fr%C3%B3is" title="Luís Fróis">Luís Fróis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> until the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the full title of Japan was <i>Dai Nippon Teikoku</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">大日本帝國</span>)</span>, meaning "the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Great Japan</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Today, the name <i>Nihon-koku/Nippon-koku</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">日本国</span>)</span> is used as a formal modern-day equivalent with the meaning of "the State of Japan". Countries like Japan whose long form does not contain a descriptive designation are generally given a name appended by the character <i>koku</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">国</span>)</span>, meaning "country", "nation" or "state".</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_ancient_history">Prehistory and ancient history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Jimmu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="674" data-file-width="438" height="339" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Emperor_Jimmu.jpg/220px-Emperor_Jimmu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Emperor_Jimmu.jpg/330px-Emperor_Jimmu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6b/Emperor_Jimmu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Jimmu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Emperor_Jimmu" title="Emperor Jimmu">Emperor Jimmu</a> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">神武天皇</span><span style="display:none">,</span> <i>Jinmu-tennō</i></span>), the first <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor of Japan</a> dated as 660 BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kelly_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-kelly-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Understanding_Japanese_Religion_p._145_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Understanding_Japanese_Religion_p._145-36">[36]</a></sup>—in modern Japan his accession is marked as <a href="/wiki/National_Foundation_Day" title="National Foundation Day">National Foundation Day</a> on February 11</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Paleolithic" title="Japanese Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> culture around 30,000 BC constitutes the first known habitation of the Japanese archipelago. This was followed from around 14,000 BC (the start of the <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Dmon_period" title="Jōmon period">Jōmon period</a>) by a <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> semi-sedentary <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherer</a> culture characterized by <a href="/wiki/Pit-house" title="Pit-house">pit dwelling</a> and rudimentary agriculture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> including by ancestors of contemporary <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yamato_people" title="Yamato people">Yamato people</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Decorated clay vessels from this period are some of the oldest surviving examples of pottery in the world. Around 300 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period#Origin_of_the_Yayoi_people" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi people</a> began to enter the Japanese islands, intermingling with the Jōmon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yayoi_period" title="Yayoi period">Yayoi period</a>, starting around 500 BC, saw the introduction of practices like wet-<a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a> farming,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> a new style of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a>, introduced from China and Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan first appears in written history in the Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Han" title="Book of Han">Book of Han</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Records_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Records of the Three Kingdoms">Records of the Three Kingdoms</a></i>, the most powerful kingdom on the archipelago during the 3rd century was called <a href="/wiki/Yamatai" title="Yamatai">Yamataikoku</a>. Buddhism was introduced to Japan from <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> and was promoted by <a href="/wiki/Prince_Sh%C5%8Dtoku" title="Prince Shōtoku">Prince Shōtoku</a>, but the subsequent development of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Japanese Buddhism</a> was primarily influenced by China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Despite early resistance, Buddhism was promoted by the ruling class and gained widespread acceptance beginning in the <a href="/wiki/Asuka_period" title="Asuka period">Asuka period</a> (592–710).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Nara_period" title="Nara period">Nara period</a> (710–784) of the 8th century marked an emergence of the centralized Japanese state centered on the Imperial Court in <a href="/wiki/Heij%C5%8D_Palace" title="Heijō Palace">Heijō-kyō</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Nara_period" title="Nara period">Nara period</a> is characterized by the appearance of a nascent <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">literature</a> as well as the development of Buddhist-inspired art and <a href="/wiki/Historic_Monuments_of_Ancient_Nara" title="Historic Monuments of Ancient Nara">architecture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> epidemic of 735–737 is believed to have killed as much as one-third of Japan's population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> In 784, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Kanmu" title="Emperor Kanmu">Emperor Kanmu</a> moved the capital from Nara to <a href="/wiki/Nagaoka-ky%C5%8D" title="Nagaoka-kyō">Nagaoka-kyō</a>, then to <a href="/wiki/Heian-ky%C5%8D" title="Heian-kyō">Heian-kyō</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>) in 794.</p>
<p>This marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Heian_period" title="Heian period">Heian period</a> (794–1185), during which a distinctly indigenous Japanese culture emerged, noted for its <a href="/wiki/Japanese_art" title="Japanese art">art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_poetry" title="Japanese poetry">poetry</a> and prose. <a href="/wiki/Murasaki_Shikibu" title="Murasaki Shikibu">Murasaki Shikibu</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> and the lyrics of Japan's national anthem "<a href="/wiki/Kimigayo" title="Kimigayo">Kimigayo</a>" were written during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> began to spread during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heian_era" title="Heian era">Heian era</a> chiefly through two major sects, <a href="/wiki/Tendai" title="Tendai">Tendai</a> by <a href="/wiki/Saich%C5%8D" title="Saichō">Saichō</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shingon" title="Shingon">Shingon</a> by <a href="/wiki/K%C5%ABkai" title="Kūkai">Kūkai</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pure_Land_Buddhism" title="Pure Land Buddhism">Pure Land Buddhism</a> (<a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Ddo-sh%C5%AB" title="Jōdo-shū">Jōdo-shū</a>, <a href="/wiki/J%C5%8Ddo_Shinsh%C5%AB" title="Jōdo Shinshū">Jōdo Shinshū</a>) became greatly popular in the latter half of the 11th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feudal_era">Feudal era</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1461" data-file-width="3184" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg/220px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg/330px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg/440px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Samurai warriors facing Mongols during the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions of Japan</a>; <a href="/wiki/Takezaki_Suenaga" title="Takezaki Suenaga">Suenaga</a>, 1293</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan's feudal era was characterized by the emergence and dominance of a ruling class of warriors, the <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a>. In 1185, following the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Taira_clan" title="Taira clan">Taira clan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Genpei_War" title="Genpei War">Genpei War</a>, sung in the epic <a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Heike" title="The Tale of the Heike">Tale of Heike</a>, samurai <a href="/wiki/Minamoto_no_Yoritomo" title="Minamoto no Yoritomo">Minamoto no Yoritomo</a> was appointed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shogun" title="Shogun">shogun</a> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Go-Toba" title="Emperor Go-Toba">Emperor Go-Toba</a>, and Yoritomo established a base of power in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamakura,_Kanagawa" title="Kamakura, Kanagawa">Kamakura</a>. After his death, the <a href="/wiki/H%C5%8Dj%C5%8D_clan" title="Hōjō clan">Hōjō clan</a> came to power as regents for the shoguns. The <a href="/wiki/Zen" title="Zen">Zen</a> school of Buddhism was introduced from China in the <a href="/wiki/Kamakura_period" title="Kamakura period">Kamakura period</a> (1185–1333) and became popular among the samurai class.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kamakura_shogunate" title="Kamakura shogunate">Kamakura shogunate</a> repelled <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">Mongol invasions</a> in 1274 and 1281, but was eventually <a href="/wiki/Kenmu_Restoration" title="Kenmu Restoration">overthrown by Emperor Go-Daigo</a>. <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Go-Daigo" title="Emperor Go-Daigo">Emperor Go-Daigo</a> was himself defeated by <a href="/wiki/Ashikaga_Takauji" title="Ashikaga Takauji">Ashikaga Takauji</a> in 1336.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg/220px-%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg/330px-%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg/440px-%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%EF%BC%B3%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%8D%EF%BD%95%EF%BD%92%EF%BD%81%EF%BD%89.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">Samurai</a> could <a href="/wiki/Kiri-sute_gomen" title="Kiri-sute gomen">kill a commoner</a> for the slightest insult and were widely feared by the Japanese population. <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>, 1798</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ashikaga Takauji established the shogunate in Muromachi, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kyoto,_Kyoto" title="Kyoto, Kyoto">Kyoto</a>. This was the start of the <a href="/wiki/Muromachi_period" title="Muromachi period">Muromachi period</a> (1336–1573). The <a href="/wiki/Ashikaga_shogunate" title="Ashikaga shogunate">Ashikaga shogunate</a> achieved glory at the age of <a href="/wiki/Ashikaga_Yoshimitsu" title="Ashikaga Yoshimitsu">Ashikaga Yoshimitsu</a>, and the culture based on Zen Buddhism ( the art of <i><a href="/wiki/Miyabi" title="Miyabi">Miyabi</a></i>) prospered. This evolved to <a href="/wiki/Higashiyama_period" title="Higashiyama period">Higashiyama Culture</a>, and prospered until the 16th century. On the other hand, the succeeding Ashikaga shogunate failed to control the feudal warlords (<i><a href="/wiki/Daimy%C5%8D" title="Daimyō">daimyōs</a></i>) and a civil war (the <a href="/wiki/%C5%8Cnin_War" title="Ōnin War">Ōnin War</a>) began in 1467, opening the century-long <a href="/wiki/Sengoku_period" title="Sengoku period">Sengoku period</a> ("Warring States").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 16th century, traders and <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a> from <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a> reached Japan for the first time, initiating direct <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">commercial</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nanban_art" title="Nanban art">cultural</a> exchange between Japan and the West. This allowed <a href="/wiki/Oda_Nobunaga" title="Oda Nobunaga">Oda Nobunaga</a> to obtain European technology and firearms, which he used to conquer many other <i>daimyōs</i>. His consolidation of power began what was known as the <a href="/wiki/Azuchi%E2%80%93Momoyama_period" title="Azuchi–Momoyama period">Azuchi–Momoyama period</a> (1573–1603). After Nobunaga was assassinated in 1582 by <a href="/wiki/Akechi_Mitsuhide" title="Akechi Mitsuhide">Akechi Mitsuhide</a>, his successor <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyoshi" title="Toyotomi Hideyoshi">Toyotomi Hideyoshi</a> unified the nation in 1590 and launched <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasions_of_Korea_(1592%E2%80%9398)" title="Japanese invasions of Korea (1592–98)">two unsuccessful invasions of Korea in 1592 and 1597</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_Ieyasu" title="Tokugawa Ieyasu">Tokugawa Ieyasu</a> served as regent for Hideyoshi's <a href="/wiki/Toyotomi_Hideyori" title="Toyotomi Hideyori">son</a> and used his position to gain political and military support. When open war broke out, Ieyasu defeated rival clans in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sekigahara" title="Battle of Sekigahara">Battle of Sekigahara</a> in 1600. Tokugawa Ieyasu was appointed shogun by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Go-Y%C5%8Dzei" title="Emperor Go-Yōzei">Emperor Go-Yōzei</a> in 1603 and established the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a> in <a href="/wiki/Edo" title="Edo">Edo</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The Tokugawa shogunate enacted measures including <i><a href="/wiki/Buke_shohatto" title="Buke shohatto">buke shohatto</a></i>, as a code of conduct to control the autonomous <i>daimyōs</i>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and in 1639 the isolationist <i><a href="/wiki/Sakoku" title="Sakoku">sakoku</a></i> ("closed country") policy that spanned the two and a half centuries of tenuous political unity known as the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> (1603–1868).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The study of Western sciences, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Rangaku" title="Rangaku">rangaku</a></i>, continued through contact with the Dutch enclave at <a href="/wiki/Dejima" title="Dejima">Dejima</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>. The Edo period also gave rise to <i><a href="/wiki/Kokugaku" title="Kokugaku">kokugaku</a></i> ("national studies"), the study of Japan by the Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meiji_tenno1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1203" data-file-width="879" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Meiji_tenno1.jpg/170px-Meiji_tenno1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Meiji_tenno1.jpg/255px-Meiji_tenno1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Meiji_tenno1.jpg/340px-Meiji_tenno1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meiji_tenno1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Emperor_Meiji" title="Emperor Meiji">Emperor Meiji</a> (1868–1912), in whose name imperial rule was <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">restored</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Tokugawa_shogunate" title="Tokugawa shogunate">Tokugawa shogunate</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On March 31, 1854, <a href="/wiki/Matthew_C._Perry" title="Matthew C. Perry">Commodore Matthew Perry</a> and the "<a href="/wiki/Black_Ships" title="Black Ships">Black Ships</a>" of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> forced the opening of Japan to the outside world with the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Kanagawa" title="Convention of Kanagawa">Convention of Kanagawa</a>. Subsequent similar treaties with Western countries in the <a href="/wiki/Bakumatsu" title="Bakumatsu">Bakumatsu</a> period brought economic and political crises. The resignation of the shogun led to the <a href="/wiki/Boshin_War" title="Boshin War">Boshin War</a> and the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Abolition_of_the_han_system" title="Abolition of the han system">centralized state</a> nominally unified under the Emperor (the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Plunging itself through an active process of Westernization during the Meiji Restoration in 1868, Japan adopted Western political, judicial and military institutions and Western cultural influences integrated with its traditional culture for modern industrialization. The <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Japan" title="Cabinet of Japan">Cabinet</a> organized the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Privy_Council_(Japan)" title="Privy Council (Japan)">Privy Council</a>, introduced the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Constitution" title="Meiji Constitution">Meiji Constitution</a>, and assembled the <a href="/wiki/National_Diet" title="National Diet">Imperial Diet</a>. The Meiji Restoration transformed the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> into an industrialized world power that pursued military conflict to expand its sphere of influence. After victories in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> (1894–1895) and the <a href="/wiki/Russo-Japanese_War" title="Russo-Japanese War">Russo-Japanese War</a> (1904–1905), Japan gained control of Taiwan, Korea and the southern half of <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Japan's population grew from 35 million in 1873 to 70 million in 1935.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="1335" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg/220px-Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg/330px-Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg/440px-Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Generals_Pyongyang_MigitaToshihide_October1894.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chinese generals surrendering to the Japanese in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">Sino-Japanese War</a> of 1894–1895</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> enabled Japan, on the side of the victorious <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allies</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Japan_during_World_War_I" title="Japan during World War I">widen its influence and territorial holdings in Asia</a>. The early 20th century saw a brief period of "<a href="/wiki/Taish%C5%8D_period" title="Taishō period">Taishō democracy (1912–1926)</a>" but the 1920s saw a fragile democracy buckle under a political shift towards <a href="/wiki/Statism_in_Sh%C5%8Dwa_Japan" title="Statism in Shōwa Japan">fascism</a>, the passing of <a href="/wiki/Peace_Preservation_Law#Public_Security_Preservation_Law_of_1925" title="Peace Preservation Law">laws against political dissent</a> and a series of attempted <a href="/wiki/May_15_Incident" title="May 15 Incident">coups</a>. The subsequent "<a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_period" title="Shōwa period">Shōwa period</a>" initially saw the power of the military increased and brought about Japanese <a href="/wiki/Expansionism" title="Expansionism">expansionism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_militarism" title="Japanese militarism">militarization</a> along with the totalitarianism and ultranationalism that are a part of fascist ideology. In 1931 Japan invaded and occupied <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> and following <a href="/wiki/Lytton_Report" title="Lytton Report">international condemnation of this occupation</a>, Japan resigned from the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> in 1933. In 1936, Japan signed the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Comintern_Pact" title="Anti-Comintern Pact">Anti-Comintern Pact</a> with <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Germany</a> and the 1940 <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">Tripartite Pact</a> made it one of the Axis Powers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In 1941, following its defeat in the brief <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Japanese_Border_War_(1939)" title="Soviet–Japanese Border War (1939)">Soviet–Japanese Border War</a>, Japan negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Soviet%E2%80%93Japanese_Neutrality_Pact" title="Soviet–Japanese Neutrality Pact">Soviet–Japanese Neutrality Pact</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> which lasted until 1945 with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet invasion of Manchuria">Soviet invasion of Manchuria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Umezu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="727" data-file-width="687" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Umezu.jpg/220px-Umezu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Umezu.jpg/330px-Umezu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Umezu.jpg/440px-Umezu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Umezu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Japanese officials surrendering to the Allies on September 2, 1945, in Tokyo Bay, ending World War II</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Empire of Japan invaded other parts of China in 1937, precipitating the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a> (1937–1945). The Imperial Japanese Army swiftly captured the capital <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> and conducted the <a href="/wiki/Nanking_Massacre" title="Nanking Massacre">Nanking Massacre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> In 1940, the Empire <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_French_Indochina" title="Japanese invasion of French Indochina">invaded French Indochina</a>, after which the United States placed an oil embargo on Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> On December 7–8, 1941, Japanese forces carried out surprise <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attacks on Pearl Harbor</a>, British forces in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Malaya" title="Battle of Malaya">Malaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bombing_of_Singapore_(1941)" title="Bombing of Singapore (1941)">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hong_Kong" title="Battle of Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_declaration_of_war_on_the_United_States_and_the_British_Empire" title="Japanese declaration of war on the United States and the British Empire">declared war on the United States and the British Empire</a>, bringing the United States and the United Kingdom into <a href="/wiki/Pacific_War" title="Pacific War">World War II in the Pacific</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> After Allied victories across the Pacific during the next four years, which culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet invasion of Manchuria">Soviet invasion of Manchuria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_bombings_of_Hiroshima_and_Nagasaki" title="Atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki">atomic bombings of Hiroshima and Nagasaki</a> in 1945, Japan agreed to an <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">unconditional surrender</a> on August 15.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The war cost Japan, <a href="/wiki/Greater_East_Asia_Co-Prosperity_Sphere" title="Greater East Asia Co-Prosperity Sphere">its colonies</a>, China and the war's other combatants tens of millions of lives and left much of Japan's industry and infrastructure destroyed. The <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> (led by the United States) repatriated millions of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_diaspora" title="Japanese diaspora">ethnic Japanese</a> from colonies and military camps throughout Asia, largely eliminating the Japanese empire and restoring the independence of its conquered territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The Allies also convened the <a href="/wiki/International_Military_Tribunal_for_the_Far_East" title="International Military Tribunal for the Far East">International Military Tribunal for the Far East</a> on May 3, 1946 to prosecute some Japanese leaders for <a href="/wiki/Japanese_war_crimes" title="Japanese war crimes">war crimes</a>. However, the <a href="/wiki/Unit_731" title="Unit 731">bacteriological research units</a> and members of the imperial family involved in the war were exonerated from criminal prosecutions by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Commander_for_the_Allied_Powers" title="Supreme Commander for the Allied Powers">Supreme Commander for the Allied Powers</a> despite calls for the trial of both groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1947, Japan adopted a new <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">constitution</a> emphasizing liberal democratic practices. The <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Japan" title="Occupation of Japan">Allied occupation</a> ended with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_San_Francisco" title="Treaty of San Francisco">Treaty of San Francisco</a> in 1952<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and Japan was granted membership in the United Nations in 1956. Japan later achieved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_post-war_economic_miracle" title="Japanese post-war economic miracle">rapid growth</a> to become the second-largest economy in the world, until surpassed by China in 2010. This ended in the mid-1990s when Japan suffered a <a href="/wiki/Japanese_asset_price_bubble" title="Japanese asset price bubble">major recession</a>. In the beginning of the 21st century, positive growth has signaled a gradual economic recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> On March 11, 2011, Japan suffered one of the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">largest earthquakes in its recorded history</a>; this triggered the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a>, one of the worst disasters in the history of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes-tsunami_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-tsunami-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Japan" title="Geography of Japan">Geography of Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Japan" title="Geology of Japan">Geology of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2092" data-file-width="1638" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg/220px-Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg/330px-Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg/440px-Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Satellite_View_of_Japan_1999.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">Japanese archipelago</a> as seen from satellite</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan has a total of 6,852 islands extending along the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_coast" title="Pacific coast">Pacific coast</a> of East Asia. The country, including all of the islands it controls, lies between latitudes 24° and 46°N, and longitudes 122° and 146°E. The main islands, from north to south, are <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>, which include <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Island" title="Okinawa Island">Okinawa</a>, are a chain to the south of <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>. Together they are often known as the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">Japanese archipelago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>About 73 percent of Japan is forested, mountainous and unsuitable for <a href="/wiki/Agriculture,_forestry,_and_fishing_in_Japan" title="Agriculture, forestry, and fishing in Japan">agricultural</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_Japan" title="Manufacturing in Japan">industrial</a> or <a href="/wiki/Housing_in_Japan" title="Housing in Japan">residential</a> use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> As a result, the habitable zones, mainly located in coastal areas, have extremely high population densities. Japan is one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_population_density" title="List of countries by population density">most densely populated countries</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>The islands of Japan are located in a <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic</a> zone on the <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Fire" title="Ring of Fire">Pacific Ring of Fire</a>. They are primarily the result of large oceanic movements occurring over hundreds of millions of years from the mid-Silurian to the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Subduction" title="Subduction">subduction</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate" title="Philippine Sea Plate">Philippine Sea Plate</a> beneath the continental <a href="/wiki/Amurian_Plate" title="Amurian Plate">Amurian Plate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Plate" title="Okinawa Plate">Okinawa Plate</a> to the south, and subduction of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Plate" title="Pacific Plate">Pacific Plate</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Okhotsk_Plate" title="Okhotsk Plate">Okhotsk Plate</a> to the north. The <a href="/wiki/Boso_Triple_Junction" title="Boso Triple Junction">Boso Triple Junction</a> off the coast of Japan is a triple junction where the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American Plate</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Plate" title="Pacific Plate">Pacific Plate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Sea_Plate" title="Philippine Sea Plate">Philippine Sea Plate</a> meets. Japan was originally attached to the eastern coast of the Eurasian continent. The subducting plates pulled Japan eastward, opening the <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a> around 15 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan has 108 active volcanoes. During the twentieth century several new volcanoes emerged, including <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa-shinzan" title="Shōwa-shinzan">Shōwa-shinzan</a> on Hokkaido and <a href="/wiki/My%C5%8Djin-sh%C5%8D" title="Myōjin-shō">Myōjin-shō</a> off the <a href="/wiki/Bayonnaise_Rocks" title="Bayonnaise Rocks">Bayonnaise Rocks</a> in the Pacific. Destructive earthquakes, often resulting in <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunami</a>, occur several times each century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1923_Great_Kant%C5%8D_earthquake" title="1923 Great Kantō earthquake">1923 Tokyo earthquake</a> killed over 140,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> More recent major quakes are the 1995 <a href="/wiki/Great_Hanshin_earthquake" title="Great Hanshin earthquake">Great Hanshin earthquake</a> and the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">2011 Tōhoku earthquake</a>, a 9.1-magnitude<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS9.1_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS9.1-78">[78]</a></sup> quake which hit Japan on March 11, 2011 and triggered a large tsunami.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes-tsunami_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-tsunami-71">[71]</a></sup> Japan is substantially prone to earthquakes, tsunami and volcanoes due to its location along the Pacific Ring of Fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> It has the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_disaster_risk" title="List of countries by natural disaster risk">15th highest natural disaster risk</a> as measured in the 2013 World Risk Index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2013_World_Risk_Report_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-2013_World_Risk_Report-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_of_Japan" title="Climate of Japan">Climate of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2000" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg/250px-Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg/375px-Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg/500px-Cherry_blossoms_at_Yoshinoyama_01.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Cherry_blossom" title="Cherry blossom">Cherry blossoms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Yoshino" title="Mount Yoshino">Mount Yoshino</a> have been the subject of many plays and <a href="/wiki/Waka_(poetry)" title="Waka (poetry)">waka poetry</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2133" data-file-width="3200" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg/250px-Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg/375px-Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg/500px-Kongobuji_Koyasan07n3200.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Autumn <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maple_tree" title="Maple tree">maple leaves</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Momiji" title="Momiji">momiji</a>) at <a href="/wiki/Kong%C5%8Dbu-ji" title="Kongōbu-ji">Kongōbu-ji</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mount_K%C5%8Dya" title="Mount Kōya">Mount Kōya</a>, a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The climate of Japan is predominantly temperate, but varies greatly from north to south. Japan's geographical features divide it into six principal climatic zones: <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Highland_(Japan)" title="Central Highland (Japan)">Central Highland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seto_Inland_Sea" title="Seto Inland Sea">Seto Inland Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a>. The northernmost zone, Hokkaido, has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a> with long, cold winters and very warm to cool summers. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">Precipitation</a> is not heavy, but the islands usually develop deep snowbanks in the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the Sea of Japan zone on Honshu's west coast, northwest winter winds bring heavy snowfall. In the summer, the region is cooler than the Pacific area, though it sometimes experiences extremely hot temperatures because of the <a href="/wiki/Foehn_wind" title="Foehn wind">foehn</a>. The Central Highland has a typical inland humid continental climate, with large temperature differences between summer and winter seasons, as well as large diurnal variation; precipitation is light, though winters are usually snowy. The mountains of the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%ABgoku_region" title="Chūgoku region">Chūgoku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a> regions shelter the <a href="/wiki/Seto_Inland_Sea" title="Seto Inland Sea">Seto Inland Sea</a> from seasonal winds, bringing mild weather year-round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Pacific coast features a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical" title="Humid subtropical">humid subtropical</a> climate that experiences milder winters with occasional snowfall and hot, humid summers because of the southeast seasonal wind. The Ryukyu Islands have a <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropical climate</a>, with warm winters and hot summers. Precipitation is very heavy, especially during the rainy season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>The average winter temperature in Japan is 5.1 °C (41.2 °F) and the average summer temperature is 25.2 °C (77.4 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The highest temperature ever measured in Japan 41.0 °C (105.8 °F) was recorded on August 12, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The main <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_rainy_season" title="East Asian rainy season">rainy season</a> begins in early May in Okinawa, and the rain front gradually moves north until reaching Hokkaido in late July. In most of Honshu, the rainy season begins before the middle of June and lasts about six weeks. In late summer and early autumn, <a href="/wiki/Typhoon" title="Typhoon">typhoons</a> often bring heavy rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-climate_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-climate-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Japan" title="Wildlife of Japan">Wildlife of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg/220px-Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg/330px-Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg/440px-Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jigokudani_hotspring_in_Nagano_Japan_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Japanese_macaque" title="Japanese macaque">Japanese macaques</a> at <a href="/wiki/Jigokudani_Monkey_Park" title="Jigokudani Monkey Park">Jigokudani hot spring</a> are notable for visiting the spa in the winter</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan has nine forest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecoregions_in_Japan" title="Ecoregions in Japan">ecoregions</a> which reflect the climate and geography of the islands. They range from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">subtropical moist broadleaf forests</a> in the Ryūkyū and <a href="/wiki/Bonin_Islands" title="Bonin Islands">Bonin Islands</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forests" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forests">temperate broadleaf and mixed forests</a> in the mild climate regions of the main islands, to <a href="/wiki/Temperate_coniferous_forest" title="Temperate coniferous forest">temperate coniferous forests</a> in the cold, winter portions of the northern islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Japan has over 90,000 species of <a href="/wiki/Wildlife_of_Japan" title="Wildlife of Japan">wildlife</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">brown bear</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_macaque" title="Japanese macaque">Japanese macaque</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_raccoon_dog" title="Japanese raccoon dog">Japanese raccoon dog</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Large_Japanese_field_mouse" title="Large Japanese field mouse">Large Japanese Field Mouse</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_giant_salamander" title="Japanese giant salamander">Japanese giant salamander</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> A large network of <a href="/wiki/List_of_national_parks_of_Japan" title="List of national parks of Japan">national parks</a> has been established to protect important areas of flora and fauna as well as thirty-seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramsar_sites_in_Japan" title="Ramsar sites in Japan">Ramsar wetland sites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites_in_Japan" title="World Heritage Sites in Japan">Four sites</a> have been inscribed on the UNESCO World Heritage List for their outstanding natural value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unesco1_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_in_Japan" title="Environmental issues in Japan">Environmental issues in Japan</a></div>
<p>In the period of rapid economic growth after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, environmental policies were downplayed by the government and industrial corporations; as a result, <a href="/wiki/Four_Big_Pollution_Diseases_of_Japan" title="Four Big Pollution Diseases of Japan">environmental pollution</a> was widespread in the 1950s and 1960s. Responding to rising concern about the problem, the government introduced several environmental protection laws in 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">oil crisis in 1973</a> also encouraged the efficient use of energy because of Japan's lack of natural resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Current environmental issues include urban air pollution (<a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a>, suspended particulate matter, and toxics), <a href="/wiki/Waste_management" title="Waste management">waste management</a>, water <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nature_conservation" title="Nature conservation">nature conservation</a>, climate change, chemical management and international co-operation for conservation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of June 2015, more than 40 coal-fired power plants are planned or under construction in Japan. The NGO Climate Action Network announced Japan as the winner of its "Fossil of the Day" award for "doing the most to block progress on climate action".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan ranks 39th in the 2016 <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Performance_Index" title="Environmental Performance Index">Environmental Performance Index</a>, which measures a nation's commitment to environmental sustainability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> As a signatory of the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> and host of the 1997 conference that created it, Japan is under treaty obligation to reduce its carbon dioxide emissions and to take other steps to curb climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Japan" title="Politics of Japan">Politics of Japan</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:left; float:right; margin-right:9px; margin-left:2px;">
<tr>
<td style="text-align:left;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg"><img alt="Emperor Akihito cropped 2 Barack Obama Emperor Akihito and Empress Michiko 20140424 1.jpg" data-file-height="930" data-file-width="662" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg/126px-Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg/189px-Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg/252px-Emperor_Akihito_cropped_2_Barack_Obama_Emperor_Akihito_and_Empress_Michiko_20140424_1.jpg 2x" width="126"/></a></td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Government of Japan</a></div>
<p>Japan is a <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Japan" title="Emperor of Japan">Emperor</a> is a ceremonial figurehead and is defined by the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">constitution</a> as "the symbol of the State and of the unity of the people". Executive power is wielded chiefly by the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Japan" title="Prime Minister of Japan">Prime Minister</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_of_Japan" title="Cabinet of Japan">cabinet</a>. Sovereignty is vested in the Japanese people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan's legislative body is the <a href="/wiki/National_Diet" title="National Diet">National Diet</a>, seated in <a href="/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo" title="Chiyoda, Tokyo">Chiyoda, Tokyo</a>. The Diet is a <a href="/wiki/Bicameralism" title="Bicameralism">bicameral</a> body, comprising the lower <a href="/wiki/House_of_Representatives_(Japan)" title="House of Representatives (Japan)">House of Representatives</a> with 475 seats, elected by popular vote every four years or when dissolved; and the upper <a href="/wiki/House_of_Councillors_(Japan)" title="House of Councillors (Japan)">House of Councillors</a> with 242 seats, whose popularly elected members serve six-year terms. There is <a href="/wiki/Universal_suffrage" title="Universal suffrage">universal suffrage</a> for adults over 18 years of age,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tst-_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-tst--97">[97]</a></sup> with a <a href="/wiki/Secret_ballot" title="Secret ballot">secret ballot</a> for all elected offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Constitution_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Constitution-96">[96]</a></sup> The Diet is dominated by the social liberal <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(Japan)" title="Democratic Party (Japan)">Democratic Party of Japan</a> (DP) and the conservative <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democratic_Party_(Japan)" title="Liberal Democratic Party (Japan)">Liberal Democratic Party</a> (LDP). The LDP has enjoyed near-continuous electoral success since 1955, except for brief periods between 1993 and 1994 and from 2009 to 2012. As of May 2017, it holds 294 seats in the lower house and 121 seats in the upper house.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1726" data-file-width="2561" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg/220px-Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg/330px-Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg/440px-Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diet_of_Japan_Kokkai_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/National_Diet_Building" title="National Diet Building">National Diet Building</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Prime Minister of Japan is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a> and is <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Investiture" title="Imperial Investiture">appointed</a> by the Emperor after being designated by the Diet from among its members. The Prime Minister is the head of the Cabinet and he appoints and dismisses the <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_State" title="Minister of State">Ministers of State</a>. Following the LDP's landslide victory in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_general_election,_2012" title="Japanese general election, 2012">2012 general election</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe" title="Shinzō Abe">Shinzō Abe</a> replaced <a href="/wiki/Yoshihiko_Noda" title="Yoshihiko Noda">Yoshihiko Noda</a> as the Prime Minister on December 26, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historically influenced by <a href="/wiki/Chinese_law" title="Chinese law">Chinese law</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Japan" title="Law of Japan">Japanese legal system</a> developed independently during the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> through texts such as <i><a href="/wiki/Kujikata_Osadamegaki" title="Kujikata Osadamegaki">Kujikata Osadamegaki</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> However, since the late 19th century the <a href="/wiki/Judicial_system_of_Japan" title="Judicial system of Japan">judicial system</a> has been largely based on the <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> of Europe, notably Germany. For example, in 1896, the Japanese government established a civil code based on a draft of the German <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCrgerliches_Gesetzbuch" title="Bürgerliches Gesetzbuch">Bürgerliches Gesetzbuch</a>; with the code remaining in effect with post–World War II modifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Statutory law originates in Japan's legislature and has the <a href="/wiki/Rubber_stamp_(politics)" title="Rubber stamp (politics)">rubber stamp</a> of the Emperor. Japan's court system is divided into four basic tiers: the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Japan" title="Supreme Court of Japan">Supreme Court</a> and three levels of lower courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The main body of Japanese statutory law is called the <a href="/wiki/Six_Codes" title="Six Codes">Six Codes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Japan" title="Administrative divisions of Japan">Administrative divisions of Japan</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prefectures_of_Japan" title="Prefectures of Japan">Prefectures of Japan</a></div>
<p>Japan consists of 47 prefectures, each overseen by an elected <a href="/wiki/Governor_(Japan)" title="Governor (Japan)">governor</a>, legislature and administrative bureaucracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Each prefecture is further divided into cities, towns and villages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The nation is currently undergoing administrative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merger_and_dissolution_of_municipalities_of_Japan" title="Merger and dissolution of municipalities of Japan">reorganization by merging</a> many of the cities, towns and villages with each other. This process will reduce the number of sub-prefecture administrative regions and is expected to cut administrative costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
