<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Japan" title="Foreign relations of Japan">Foreign relations of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="333" data-file-width="500" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg/220px-Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg/330px-Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg/440px-Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Liancourt_walleye_view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Liancourt_Rocks" title="Liancourt Rocks">Liancourt Rocks</a> named as Takeshima in Japan, has become an issue known as the <a href="/wiki/Liancourt_Rocks_dispute" title="Liancourt Rocks dispute">Liancourt Rocks dispute</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan has diplomatic relations with nearly all independent nations and has been an active member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nation" title="United Nation">UN</a> since December 1956. Japan is a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Group_of_Eight_(G8)" title="Group of Eight (G8)">G8</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">APEC</a>, and "<a href="/wiki/ASEAN_Free_Trade_Area#ASEAN_Plus_Three" title="ASEAN Free Trade Area">ASEAN Plus Three</a>", and is a participant in the <a href="/wiki/East_Asia_Summit" title="East Asia Summit">East Asia Summit</a>. Japan signed a security pact with <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> in March 2007<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and with <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> in October 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> It is the world's fifth largest donor of <a href="/wiki/Official_development_assistance" title="Official development assistance">official development assistance</a>, donating US$9.2 billion in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan has close ties to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Since Japan's defeat by the United States in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the two countries have maintained close economic and defense relations. The United States is a major market for Japanese exports and the primary source of Japanese imports, and is committed to defending the country, having military bases in Japan for partially that purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan contests <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>'s control of the <a href="/wiki/Kuril_Islands_dispute" title="Kuril Islands dispute">Southern Kuril Islands</a> (including Etorofu, Kunashiri, Shikotan, and the Habomai group) which were occupied by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> in 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>'s assertions concerning <a href="/wiki/Liancourt_Rocks" title="Liancourt Rocks">Liancourt Rocks</a> (Japanese: "Takeshima", Korean: "Dokdo") are acknowledged, but not accepted by Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-takeshima_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-takeshima-111">[111]</a></sup> Japan has strained relations with the <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">People's Republic of China</a> (PRC) and the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Republic of China</a> (Taiwan) over the <a href="/wiki/Senkaku_Islands" title="Senkaku Islands">Senkaku Islands</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> and with the People's Republic of China over the status of <a href="/wiki/Okinotorishima" title="Okinotorishima">Okinotorishima</a>.</p>
<p>Japan's relationship with <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a> has been strained due to Japan's treatment of Koreans during <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Japanese colonial rule</a>, particularly over the issue of <a href="/wiki/Comfort_women" title="Comfort women">comfort women</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> These women were essentially sex slaves, and although there is no exact number on how many women were subjected to this treatment, experts believe it could be in the tens or hundreds of thousands. Between 1910–1945, the Japanese government rebuilt Korean infrastructure, introduced over 800,000 Japanese immigrants onto the peninsula, and carried out a campaign of cultural suppression through efforts to ban the Korean language in schools and force Koreans to adopt Japanese names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> With the defeat of Japan and the axis after WWII in 1945, the Korean peninsula was once again independent. Despite their historical tensions, in December 2015, Japan agreed to settle the comfort women dispute with South Korea by issuing a formal apology, taking responsibility for the issue and paying money to the surviving comfort women. Today, South Korea and Japan have a stronger and more economically-driven relationship. Since the 1990s, the Korean Wave has created a large fanbase in east Asia, but most notably in Japan. Japan is the number one importer of Korean music (K-pop), television (K-dramas), and films, but this was only made possible after the South Korean government lifted the 30-year ban on cultural exchange with Japan that had been in place since 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Korean pop cultural products' success in the Japanese market is partially explained by the borrowing of Japanese ideas such as the star-marketing system and heavy promotion of new television shows and music. K-dramas such as <i>Winter Sonata</i> and <i>Coffee Prince,</i> as well as K-pop artists such as BIGBANG and SHINee are extremely popular with Japanese consumers. Most recently, South Korean President Moon Jae-in met with Japanese Prime Minister Shinzo Abe at the 2017 G-20 Summit in Hamburg, Germany to discuss the future of their relationship and specifically how to cooperate on finding solutions for North Korean aggression in the region. Both leaders restated their commitment to solving the comfort women dispute, building positive relations in the region, and pressuring China to be more assertive with North Korea as it continues to test nuclear weapons and isolate themselves further form the international community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japan_Self-Defense_Forces" title="Japan Self-Defense Forces">Japan Self-Defense Forces</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_051115-N-8492C-125_The_Japan_Maritime_Self-Defense_Force_(JMSDF)_destroyer_JDS_Kongou_(DDG_173)_sails_in_formation_with_other_JMSDF_ships_and_ships_assigned_to_the_USS_Kitty_Hawk_Carrier_Strike_Group.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/US_Navy_051115-N-8492C-125_The_Japan_Maritime_Self-Defense_Force_%28JMSDF%29_destroyer_JDS_Kongou_%28DDG_173%29_sails_in_formation_with_other_JMSDF_ships_and_ships_assigned_to_the_USS_Kitty_Hawk_Carrier_Strike_Group.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/US_Navy_051115-N-8492C-125_The_Japan_Maritime_Self-Defense_Force_%28JMSDF%29_destroyer_JDS_Kongou_%28DDG_173%29_sails_in_formation_with_other_JMSDF_ships_and_ships_assigned_to_the_USS_Kitty_Hawk_Carrier_Strike_Group.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/US_Navy_051115-N-8492C-125_The_Japan_Maritime_Self-Defense_Force_%28JMSDF%29_destroyer_JDS_Kongou_%28DDG_173%29_sails_in_formation_with_other_JMSDF_ships_and_ships_assigned_to_the_USS_Kitty_Hawk_Carrier_Strike_Group.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

<a href="/wiki/Japan_Maritime_Self-Defense_Force" title="Japan Maritime Self-Defense Force">JMSDF</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kong%C5%8D_class_destroyer" title="Kongō class destroyer"><i>Kongō</i> class destroyer</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SM3_from_JDS_Kongo.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2400" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/SM3_from_JDS_Kongo.jpg/220px-SM3_from_JDS_Kongo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/SM3_from_JDS_Kongo.jpg/330px-SM3_from_JDS_Kongo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/SM3_from_JDS_Kongo.jpg/440px-SM3_from_JDS_Kongo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JDS_Kong%C5%8D" title="JDS Kongō">JDS <i>Kongō</i> (DDG-173)</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Guided_missile_destroyer" title="Guided missile destroyer">guided missile destroyer</a>, launching a <a href="/wiki/RIM-161_Standard_Missile_3" title="RIM-161 Standard Missile 3">Standard Missile 3</a> <a href="/wiki/Anti-ballistic_missile" title="Anti-ballistic missile">anti-ballistic missile</a> in 2007</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitsubishi_F-2_landing.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1029" data-file-width="2140" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mitsubishi_F-2_landing.JPG/220px-Mitsubishi_F-2_landing.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mitsubishi_F-2_landing.JPG/330px-Mitsubishi_F-2_landing.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mitsubishi_F-2_landing.JPG/440px-Mitsubishi_F-2_landing.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">JASDF <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_F-2" title="Mitsubishi F-2">F-2</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Multirole_combat_aircraft" title="Multirole combat aircraft">multirole combat aircraft</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan maintains one of the largest military budgets of any country in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The country's military (the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Self-Defense_Forces" title="Japan Self-Defense Forces">Japan Self-Defense Forces</a>) is restricted by <a href="/wiki/Article_9_of_the_Japanese_Constitution" title="Article 9 of the Japanese Constitution">Article 9 of the Japanese Constitution</a>, which renounces Japan's right to declare war or use military force in international disputes. Accordingly, Japan's Self-Defence force is an unusual military that has never fired shots outside Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Japan is the highest-ranked Asian country in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Peace_Index" title="Global Peace Index">Global Peace Index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The military is governed by the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Defense_(Japan)" title="Ministry of Defense (Japan)">Ministry of Defense</a>, and primarily consists of the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Ground_Self-Defense_Force" title="Japan Ground Self-Defense Force">Japan Ground Self-Defense Force</a> (JGSDF), the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Maritime_Self-Defense_Force" title="Japan Maritime Self-Defense Force">Japan Maritime Self-Defense Force</a> (JMSDF) and the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Air_Self-Defense_Force" title="Japan Air Self-Defense Force">Japan Air Self-Defense Force</a> (JASDF). The Japan Maritime Self-Defense Force (JMSDF) is a regular participant in <a href="/wiki/Exercise_RIMPAC" title="Exercise RIMPAC">RIMPAC</a> maritime exercises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The forces have been recently used in peacekeeping operations; the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Iraq_Reconstruction_and_Support_Group" title="Japanese Iraq Reconstruction and Support Group">deployment of troops to Iraq</a> marked the first overseas use of Japan's military since World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iraq_deployment_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iraq_deployment-121">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japan_Business_Federation" title="Japan Business Federation">Japan Business Federation</a> has called on the government to lift the ban on arms exports so that Japan can join multinational projects such as the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Strike_Fighter_program" title="Joint Strike Fighter program">Joint Strike Fighter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 21st century is witnessing a rapid change in global power balance along with globalization. The security environment around Japan has become increasingly severe as represented by nuclear and missile development by <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. Transnational threats grounded on technological progress including international terrorism and cyber attacks are also increasing their significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan.27s_Security_Policy_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japan.27s_Security_Policy-123">[123]</a></sup> Japan, including its Self Defense Forces, has contributed to the maximum extent possible to the efforts to maintain and restore international peace and security, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_peacekeeping" title="UN peacekeeping">UN peacekeeping</a> operations. Building on the ongoing efforts as a peaceful state, the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Japan" title="Government of Japan">Government of Japan</a> has been making various efforts on its security policy which include: the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/National_Security_Council_(Japan)" title="National Security Council (Japan)">National Security Council</a> (NSC), the adoption of the National Security Strategy (NSS), and the National Defense Program Guidelines (NDPG).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan.27s_Security_Policy_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Japan.27s_Security_Policy-123">[123]</a></sup> These efforts are made based on the belief that Japan, as a "Proactive Contributor to Peace", needs to contribute more actively to the peace and stability of the region and the international community, while coordinating with other countries including its ally, the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan.27s_Security_Policy_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-Japan.27s_Security_Policy-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan has close economic and military relations with the United States; the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Mutual_Cooperation_and_Security_between_the_United_States_and_Japan" title="Treaty of Mutual Cooperation and Security between the United States and Japan">US-Japan security alliance</a> acts as the cornerstone of the nation's foreign policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> A member state of the United Nations since 1956, Japan has served as a non-permanent <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">Security Council</a> member for a <a href="/wiki/List_of_members_of_the_United_Nations_Security_Council#List_by_number_of_years_as_Security_Council_member" title="List of members of the United Nations Security Council">total of 20 years</a>, most recently for 2009 and 2010. It is one of the <a href="/wiki/G4_nations" title="G4 nations">G4 nations</a> seeking permanent membership in the Security Council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2014, Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Shinz%C5%8D_Abe" title="Shinzō Abe">Shinzō Abe</a> said Japan wanted to shed the passiveness it has maintained since the end of World War II and take more responsibility for regional security. Abe said Japan wanted to play a key role and offered neighboring countries Japan's support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JapanAsia_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-JapanAsia-126">[126]</a></sup> In recent years, they have been engaged in international <a href="/wiki/Peacekeeping" title="Peacekeeping">peacekeeping</a> operations including the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_peacekeeping" title="United Nations peacekeeping">UN peacekeeping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Recent tensions, particularly with <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> have reignited the debate over the status of the JSDF and its relation to Japanese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> New military guidelines, announced in December 2010, will direct the JSDF away from its <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> focus on the former <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Armed_Forces" title="Soviet Armed Forces">Soviet Union</a> to a focus on <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">China</a>, especially regarding the territorial dispute over the <a href="/wiki/Senkaku_Islands" title="Senkaku Islands">Senkaku Islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MARTIN_FACKLER_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-MARTIN_FACKLER-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Japan" title="Economy of Japan">Economy of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo,_Japan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="963" data-file-width="1486" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/220px-Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/330px-Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/440px-Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bank_of_Japan_headquarters_in_Tokyo,_Japan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Japan" title="Bank of Japan">Bank of Japan</a> headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%AB%C5%8D,_Tokyo" title="Chūō, Tokyo">Chuo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg/220px-Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg/330px-Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg/440px-Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_stock_exchange.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Stock_Exchange" title="Tokyo Stock Exchange">Tokyo Stock Exchange</a>, one of the largest stock exchanges in Asia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg/220px-Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg/330px-Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg/440px-Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ginza_Wako_Clock.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ginza" title="Ginza">Ginza</a>, a luxury shopping area in Tokyo</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan is the third largest national economy in the world, after the United States and China, in terms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nominal_GDP" title="Nominal GDP">nominal GDP</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> and the fourth largest national economy in the world, after the United States, China and India, in terms of <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a>. As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Japan's <a href="/wiki/Government_debt" title="Government debt">public debt</a> was estimated at more than 230 percent of its annual gross domestic product, the largest of any nation in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> In August 2011, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moody%27s" title="Moody's">Moody's</a> rating has cut Japan's long-term sovereign debt rating one notch from Aa3 to Aa2 inline with the size of the country's deficit and borrowing level. The large budget deficits and government debt since the 2009 global recession and followed by the earthquake and tsunami in March 2011 caused the rating downgrade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Service_sector" title="Service sector">service sector</a> accounts for three quarters of the gross domestic product.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan has a large industrial capacity, and is home to some of the largest and most technologically advanced producers of motor vehicles, <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Machine_tool" title="Machine tool">machine tools</a>, steel and nonferrous metals, ships, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_substance" title="Chemical substance">chemical substances</a>, textiles, and <a href="/wiki/Food_processing" title="Food processing">processed foods</a>. <a href="/wiki/Agriculture,_forestry,_and_fishing_in_Japan" title="Agriculture, forestry, and fishing in Japan">Agricultural businesses in Japan</a> cultivate 13 percent of Japan's land, and Japan accounts for nearly 15 percent of the global fish catch, second only to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup> As of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Japan's labor force consisted of some 65.9 million workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup> Japan has a <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_unemployment_rate" title="List of countries by unemployment rate">low unemployment rate</a> of around four percent. Some 20 million people, around 17 per cent of the population, were below the poverty line in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Housing_in_Japan" title="Housing in Japan">Housing in Japan</a> is characterized by limited land supply in urban areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan's exports amounted to US$4,210 per capita in 2005. As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Japan's main export markets were the United States (20.2 percent), China (17.5 percent), South Korea (7.1 percent), Hong Kong (5.6 percent) and Thailand (4.5 percent). Its main exports are transportation equipment, motor vehicles, iron and steel products, semiconductors and auto parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup> Japan's main import markets as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> were China (24.8 percent), the United States (10.5 percent), Australia (5.4 percent) and South Korea (4.1 percent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan's main imports are machinery and equipment, <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, foodstuffs (in particular beef), chemicals, textiles and raw materials for its industries. By market share measures, domestic markets are the least open of any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">OECD</a> country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd2008_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-oecd2008-138">[138]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Junichir%C5%8D_Koizumi" title="Junichirō Koizumi">Junichirō Koizumi</a>'s administration began some pro-competition reforms, and foreign investment in Japan has soared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan ranks 27th of 189 countries in the 2014 <a href="/wiki/Ease_of_doing_business_index" title="Ease of doing business index">Ease of doing business index</a> and has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_tax_revenue_as_percentage_of_GDP" title="List of countries by tax revenue as percentage of GDP">one of the smallest tax revenues</a> of the developed world. The Japanese variant of capitalism has many distinct features: <a href="/wiki/Keiretsu" title="Keiretsu">keiretsu</a> enterprises are influential, and <a href="/wiki/Permanent_employment" title="Permanent employment">lifetime employment</a> and seniority-based career advancement are relatively common in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_work_environment" title="Japanese work environment">Japanese work environment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oecd2008_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-oecd2008-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Japanese companies are known for management methods like "<a href="/wiki/The_Toyota_Way" title="The Toyota Way">The Toyota Way</a>", and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shareholder_activism" title="Shareholder activism">shareholder activism</a> is rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_history">Economic history</span></h3>
<p>Modern Japan's economic growth began in the <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a>. Some of the surviving elements of the Edo period are <a href="/wiki/Kaid%C5%8D" title="Kaidō">roads</a> and water transportation routes, as well as financial instruments such as <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures contracts</a>, banking and insurance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osaka_rice_brokers" title="Osaka rice brokers">Osaka rice brokers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> During the Meiji period from 1868, Japan expanded economically with the embrace of the <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Many of today's enterprises were founded at the time, and Japan emerged as the most developed nation in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The period of overall real economic growth from the 1960s to the 1980s has been called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_post-war_economic_miracle" title="Japanese post-war economic miracle">Japanese post-war economic miracle</a>: it averaged 7.5 percent in the 1960s and 1970s, and 3.2 percent in the 1980s and early 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Growth slowed in the 1990s during the <a href="/wiki/Lost_Decade_(Japan)" title="Lost Decade (Japan)">"Lost Decade"</a> due to after-effects of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_asset_price_bubble" title="Japanese asset price bubble">Japanese asset price bubble</a> and government policies intended to wring speculative excesses from the stock and real estate markets. Efforts to revive economic growth were unsuccessful and further hampered by the <a href="/wiki/Dot-com_bubble" title="Dot-com bubble">global slowdown in 2000</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup> The economy recovered after 2005; GDP growth for that year was 2.8 percent, surpassing the growth rates of the US and <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> during the same period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Today Japan ranks highly for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Competitiveness" title="Competitiveness">competitiveness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_freedom" title="Economic freedom">economic freedom</a>. It is ranked sixth in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Competitiveness_Report" title="Global Competitiveness Report">Global Competitiveness Report</a> for 2015–2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_fishery">Agriculture and fishery</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agriculture,_forestry,_and_fishing_in_Japan" title="Agriculture, forestry, and fishing in Japan">Agriculture, forestry, and fishing in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu,_Japan.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu%2C_Japan.JPG/220px-Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu%2C_Japan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu%2C_Japan.JPG/330px-Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu%2C_Japan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu%2C_Japan.JPG/440px-Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu%2C_Japan.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rice_Paddies_In_Aizu,_Japan.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rice_paddy" title="Rice paddy">rice paddy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Aizu" title="Aizu">Aizu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Prefecture" title="Fukushima Prefecture">Fukushima Prefecture</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Japanese agricultural sector accounts for about 1.4% of the total country's GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan_Country_Report_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japan_Country_Report-149">[149]</a></sup> Only 12% of Japan's land is suitable for cultivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-As_Farmers_Age.2C_Japan_Rethinks_Relationship_With_Food.2C_Fields_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-As_Farmers_Age.2C_Japan_Rethinks_Relationship_With_Food.2C_Fields-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trip_Report_-_Japan_Agricultural_Situation_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trip_Report_-_Japan_Agricultural_Situation-151">[151]</a></sup> Due to this lack of arable land, a system of terraces is used to farm in small areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urbanites_Help_Sustain_Japan.27s_Historic_Rice_Paddy_Terraces_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urbanites_Help_Sustain_Japan.27s_Historic_Rice_Paddy_Terraces-152">[152]</a></sup> This results in one of the world's highest levels of crop yields per unit area, with an overall agricultural self-sufficiency rate of about 50% on fewer than 56,000 square kilometres (14,000,000 <a href="/wiki/Acre" title="Acre">acres</a>) cultivated.</p>
<p>Japan's small agricultural sector, however, is also highly subsidized and protected, with government regulations that favor small-scale cultivation instead of large-scale agriculture as practiced in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-As_Farmers_Age.2C_Japan_Rethinks_Relationship_With_Food.2C_Fields_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-As_Farmers_Age.2C_Japan_Rethinks_Relationship_With_Food.2C_Fields-150">[150]</a></sup> There has been a growing concern about farming as the current farmers are aging with a difficult time finding successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_will_Japan.27s_farms_survive.3F_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-How_will_Japan.27s_farms_survive.3F-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rice accounts for almost all of Japan's cereal production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan_-_Agriculture_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japan_-_Agriculture-154">[154]</a></sup> Japan is the second-largest agricultural product importer in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan_-_Agriculture_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Japan_-_Agriculture-154">[154]</a></sup> Rice, the most protected crop, is subject to tariffs of 777.7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trip_Report_-_Japan_Agricultural_Situation_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trip_Report_-_Japan_Agricultural_Situation-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-With_fewer.2C_bigger_plots_and_fewer_part-time_farmers.2C_agriculture_could_compete_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-With_fewer.2C_bigger_plots_and_fewer_part-time_farmers.2C_agriculture_could_compete-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, Japan ranked fourth in the world in <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry_by_country" title="Fishing industry by country">tonnage of fish caught</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_review_of_fisheries_and_aquaculture_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_review_of_fisheries_and_aquaculture-156">[156]</a></sup> Japan captured 4,074,580 metric tons of fish in 2005, down from 4,987,703 tons in 2000, 9,558,615 tons in 1990, 9,864,422 tons in 1980, 8,520,397 tons in 1970, 5,583,796 tons in 1960 and 2,881,855 tons in 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fish_capture_by_country_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fish_capture_by_country-157">[157]</a></sup> In 2003, the total aquaculture production was predicted at 1,301,437 tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan_National_Aquaculture_Sector_Overview_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japan_National_Aquaculture_Sector_Overview-158">[158]</a></sup> In 2010, Japan's total fisheries production was 4,762,469 fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_fisheries_production.2C_by_capture_and_aquaculture.2C_by_country_.282010.29_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_fisheries_production.2C_by_capture_and_aquaculture.2C_by_country_.282010.29-159">[159]</a></sup> Offshore fisheries accounted for an average of 50% of the nation's total fish catches in the late 1980s although they experienced repeated ups and downs during that period.</p>
<p>Today, Japan maintains one of the world's largest fishing fleets and accounts for nearly 15% of the global catch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> prompting some claims that Japan's fishing is leading to depletion in fish stocks such as <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_tribunal_halts_Japanese_tuna_over-fishing_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_tribunal_halts_Japanese_tuna_over-fishing-161">[161]</a></sup> Japan has also sparked controversy by supporting quasi-commercial <a href="/wiki/Whaling_in_Japan" title="Whaling in Japan">whaling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japanese_whaling_.27science.27_rapped_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japanese_whaling_.27science.27_rapped-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing_in_Japan" title="Manufacturing in Japan">Manufacturing in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="600" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg/220px-Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg/330px-Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg/440px-Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toyota_Plant_Ohira_Sendai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Toyota factory in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ohira,_Miyagi" title="Ohira, Miyagi">Ohira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miyagi_Prefecture" title="Miyagi Prefecture">Miyagi Prefecture</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan's industrial sector makes up approximately 27.5% of its GDP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-163">[163]</a></sup> Japan's major industries are motor vehicles, electronics, machine tools, metals, ships, chemicals and processed foods; some major Japanese industrial companies include <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canon_Inc." title="Canon Inc.">Canon Inc.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Steel_%26_Sumitomo_Metal" title="Nippon Steel &amp; Sumitomo Metal">Nippon Steel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan is the third largest automobile producer in the world, and is home to <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, the world's largest automobile company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The Japanese consumer electronics industry, once considered the strongest in the world, is currently in a state of decline as competition arises in countries like <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> However, despite also facing similar competition from South Korea and China, the Japanese shipbuilding industry is expected to remain strong thanks to an increased focus on specialized, high-tech designs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Services">Services</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trade_and_services_in_Japan" title="Trade and services in Japan">Trade and services in Japan</a></div>
<p>Japan's service sector accounts for about three-quarters of its total economic output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan_Country_Report_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Japan_Country_Report-149">[149]</a></sup> Banking, insurance, real estate, retailing, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Japan" title="Transportation in Japan">transportation</a>, and telecommunications are all major industries, with companies such as <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_UFJ_Financial_Group" title="Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group">Mitsubishi UFJ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mizuho_Financial_Group" title="Mizuho Financial Group">Mizuho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Telegraph_and_Telephone" title="Nippon Telegraph and Telephone">NTT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Electric_Power_Company" title="Tokyo Electric Power Company">TEPCO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nomura_Holdings" title="Nomura Holdings">Nomura</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Estate_Co." title="Mitsubishi Estate Co.">Mitsubishi Estate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%86ON_(company)" title="ÆON (company)">ÆON</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitsui_Sumitomo_Insurance" title="Mitsui Sumitomo Insurance">Mitsui Sumitomo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Softbank" title="Softbank">Softbank</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Japan_Railway_Company" title="East Japan Railway Company">JR East</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seven_%26_I_Holdings_Co." title="Seven &amp; I Holdings Co.">Seven &amp; I</a>, <a href="/wiki/KDDI" title="KDDI">KDDI</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japan_Airlines" title="Japan Airlines">Japan Airlines</a> listed as some of the largest in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fortune_Global_500_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fortune_Global_500-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Four of the five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_newspapers_in_the_world_by_circulation" title="List of newspapers in the world by circulation">most circulated newspapers in the world</a> are <a href="/wiki/Japanese_newspapers" title="Japanese newspapers">Japanese newspapers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-National_Newspapers_Total_Circulation_2011_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-National_Newspapers_Total_Circulation_2011-172">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japan_Post" title="Japan Post">Japan Post</a>, one of the country's largest providers of savings and insurance services, was slated for privatization by 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Japan_govt_aims_to_list_Japan_Post_in_three_years_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Japan_govt_aims_to_list_Japan_Post_in_three_years-173">[173]</a></sup> The six major <a href="/wiki/Keiretsu" title="Keiretsu">keiretsus</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi" title="Mitsubishi">Mitsubishi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sumitomo" title="Sumitomo">Sumitomo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuyo" title="Fuyo">Fuyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mitsui" title="Mitsui">Mitsui</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dai-Ichi_Kangyo" title="Dai-Ichi Kangyo">Dai-Ichi Kangyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sanwa_Group" title="Sanwa Group">Sanwa</a> Groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism">Tourism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Japan" title="Tourism in Japan">Tourism in Japan</a></div>

</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan attracted 19.73 million international tourists in 2015 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> and increased by 21.8% to attracted 24.03 million international tourists in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Tourism from abroad is one of the few promising businesses in Japan. Foreign visitors to Japan doubled in last decade and reached 10 million people for the first time in 2013, led by increase of Asian visitors.</p>
<p>In 2008, the Japanese government has set up Japan Tourism Agency and set the initial goal to increase foreign visitors to 20 million in 2020. In 2016, having met the 20 million target, the government has revised up its target to 40 million by 2020 and to 60 million by 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Number_of_Foreign_Visitors_Surpassed_20_million_.28Japan.29_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Number_of_Foreign_Visitors_Surpassed_20_million_.28Japan.29-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan has 20 <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Himeji_Castle" title="Himeji Castle">Himeji Castle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historic_Monuments_of_Ancient_Kyoto_(Kyoto,_Uji_and_Otsu_Cities)" title="Historic Monuments of Ancient Kyoto (Kyoto, Uji and Otsu Cities)">Historic Monuments of Ancient Kyoto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historic_Monuments_of_Ancient_Nara" title="Historic Monuments of Ancient Nara">Nara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Popular tourist attractions include <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Tokyo" title="Tourism in Tokyo">Tokyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Fuji" title="Mount Fuji">Mount Fuji</a>, ski resorts such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niseko" title="Niseko">Niseko</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Prefecture" title="Okinawa Prefecture">Okinawa</a>, riding the <a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">shinkansen</a> and taking advantage of Japan's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ryokan_(Japanese_inn)" title="Ryokan (Japanese inn)">hotel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Onsen" title="Onsen">hotspring</a> network.</p>
<p>For inbound tourism, Japan was <a href="/wiki/World_Tourism_rankings" title="World Tourism rankings">ranked</a> 16th in the world in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WTO_Tourism_Highlights_2015_Edition_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-WTO_Tourism_Highlights_2015_Edition-182">[182]</a></sup> In 2009, the <i><a href="/wiki/Yomiuri_Shimbun" title="Yomiuri Shimbun">Yomiuri Shimbun</a></i> published a modern list of famous sights under the name <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heisei_Hyakkei" title="Heisei Hyakkei">Heisei Hyakkei</a></i> (the Hundred Views of the Heisei period). The <i><a href="/wiki/Travel_and_Tourism_Competitiveness_Report" title="Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Report">Travel and Tourism Competitiveness Report</a> 2017</i> ranks Japan 4th out of 141 countries overall, which was the best in Asia. Japan gained relatively high scores in almost all aspects, especially health and hygiene, safety and security, cultural resources and business travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, 24,039,053 foreign tourists visited Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Neighbouring South Korea is Japan's most important source of foreign tourists. In 2010, the 2.4 million arrivals made up 27% of the tourists visiting Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dickie_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dickie-185">[185]</a></sup> Chinese travelers are the highest spenders in Japan by country, spending an estimated 196.4 billion yen (US$2.4 billion) in 2011, or almost a quarter of total expenditure by foreign visitors, according to data from the Japan Tourism Agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Japanese government hopes to receive 40 million foreign tourists every year by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10year_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-10year-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
