<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_and_technology">Science and technology</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Science_and_technology_in_Japan" title="Science and technology in Japan">Science and technology in Japan</a></div>
<p>Japan is a leading nation in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_research" title="Scientific research">scientific research</a>, particularly in fields related to the natural sciences and engineering. The country ranks second among the most innovative countries in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Innovation_Index" title="Bloomberg Innovation Index">Bloomberg Innovation Index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Nearly 700,000 researchers share a US$130 billion <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> The amount spent on <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> relative to its gross domestic product <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_research_and_development_spending" title="List of countries by research and development spending">third highest in the world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> The country is a world leader in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_research" title="Fundamental research">fundamental scientific research</a>, having produced twenty-two <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel laureates</a> in either physics, chemistry or medicine<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> and three <a href="/wiki/Fields_Medal" title="Fields Medal">Fields medalists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japanese scientists and engineers have contributed to the advancement of agricultural sciences, electronics, <a href="/wiki/Industrial_robot" title="Industrial robot">industrial robotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, chemicals, <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life_science" title="Life science">life sciences</a> and various fields of engineering. Japan leads the world in <a href="/wiki/Robotics" title="Robotics">robotics</a> production and use, possessing more than 20% (300,000 of 1.3 million) of the world's industrial robots as of 2013<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>—though its share was historically even higher, representing one-half of all industrial robots worldwide in 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> Japan boasts the third highest number of scientists, technicians, and engineers per capita in the world with 83 scientists, technicians and engineers per 10,000 employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics_and_automotive_engineering">Electronics and automotive engineering</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1388" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg/220px-Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg/330px-Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg/440px-Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid-11-09-04-iaa-by-RalfR-108.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A plug-in <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid car</a> manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, one of the world's largest carmakers—Japan is the second-largest producer of automobiles in the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Japanese electronics and automotive manufacturing industry is well known throughout the world, and the country's electronic and automotive products account for a large share in the global market, compared to a majority of other countries. Brands such as <a href="/wiki/Fujifilm" title="Fujifilm">Fujifilm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panasonic" title="Panasonic">Panasonic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nissan" title="Nissan">Nissan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Honda" title="Honda">Honda</a> are internationally famous. It is estimated that 16% of the world's gold and 22% of the world's silver is contained in Japanese electronics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan has started a project to build the world's fastest supercomputer by the end of 2017.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aerospace">Aerospace</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2039" data-file-width="3072" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg/220px-Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg/330px-Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg/440px-Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kibo_PM_and_ELM-PS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module" title="Japanese Experiment Module">Japanese Experiment Module</a> (Kibo) at the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japan_Aerospace_Exploration_Agency" title="Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency">Japan Aerospace Exploration Agency</a> (JAXA) is Japan's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_agency" title="Space agency">space agency</a>; it conducts space, planetary, and aviation research, and leads development of rockets and satellites. It is a participant in the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module" title="Japanese Experiment Module">Japanese Experiment Module</a> (Kibo) was added to the station during <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> assembly flights in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Akatsuki_(spacecraft)" title="Akatsuki (spacecraft)">Akatsuki</a></i> was launched May 20, 2010, and achieved orbit around Venus on December 9, 2015. Japan's plans in <a href="/wiki/Space_exploration" title="Space exploration">space exploration</a> include: developing the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercury_Magnetospheric_Orbiter" title="Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter">Mercury Magnetospheric Orbiter</a></i> to be launched in 2016;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FACTS_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FACTS-202">[202]</a></sup> and building a <a href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_Moon" title="Colonization of the Moon">moon base</a> by 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 14, 2007, it launched lunar explorer <a href="/wiki/SELENE" title="SELENE">SELENE</a> (Selenological and Engineering Explorer) on an <a href="/wiki/H-IIA" title="H-IIA">H-IIA</a> (Model H2A2022) carrier rocket from <a href="/wiki/Tanegashima_Space_Center" title="Tanegashima Space Center">Tanegashima Space Center</a>. SELENE is also known as Kaguya, after the lunar princess of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Bamboo_Cutter" title="The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter">The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jaxa_nickname_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-jaxa_nickname-204">[204]</a></sup> Kaguya is the largest lunar mission since the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo program</a>. Its purpose is to gather data on the <a href="/wiki/Moon#Formation" title="Moon">moon's origin and evolution</a>. It entered a lunar orbit on October 4,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> flying at an altitude of about 100 km (62 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> The probe's mission was ended when it was deliberately crashed by JAXA into the Moon on June 11, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nobel_laureates">Nobel laureates</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_Nobel_laureates" title="List of Japanese Nobel laureates">List of Japanese Nobel laureates</a></div>
<p>Japan has received the most science <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize" title="Nobel Prize">Nobel Prizes</a> in Asia and ranked 8th in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hideki_Yukawa" title="Hideki Yukawa">Hideki Yukawa</a>, educated at <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_University" title="Kyoto University">Kyoto University</a>, was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics" title="Nobel Prize in Physics">prize in physics</a> in 1949. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sin-Itiro_Tomonaga" title="Sin-Itiro Tomonaga">Sin-Itiro Tomonaga</a> followed in 1965. Solid-state physicist <a href="/wiki/Leo_Esaki" title="Leo Esaki">Leo Esaki</a>, educated at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a>, received the prize in 1973. <a href="/wiki/Kenichi_Fukui" title="Kenichi Fukui">Kenichi Fukui</a> of Kyoto University shared the 1981 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">prize in chemistry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Susumu_Tonegawa" title="Susumu Tonegawa">Susumu Tonegawa</a>, also educated at Kyoto University, became Japan's first laureate in <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">physiology or medicine</a> in 1987. Japanese chemists took prizes in 2000 and 2001: first <a href="/wiki/Hideki_Shirakawa" title="Hideki Shirakawa">Hideki Shirakawa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Institute_of_Technology" title="Tokyo Institute of Technology">Tokyo Institute of Technology</a>) and then <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%8Dji_Noyori" title="Ryōji Noyori">Ryōji Noyori</a> (Kyoto University). In 2002, <a href="/wiki/Masatoshi_Koshiba" title="Masatoshi Koshiba">Masatoshi Koshiba</a> (University of Tokyo) and <a href="/wiki/Koichi_Tanaka" title="Koichi Tanaka">Koichi Tanaka</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tohoku_University" title="Tohoku University">Tohoku University</a>) won in physics and chemistry, respectively. <a href="/wiki/Makoto_Kobayashi_(physicist)" title="Makoto Kobayashi (physicist)">Makoto Kobayashi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toshihide_Masukawa" title="Toshihide Masukawa">Toshihide Masukawa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yoichiro_Nambu" title="Yoichiro Nambu">Yoichiro Nambu</a>, who was an American citizen when awarded, shared the physics prize and <a href="/wiki/Osamu_Shimomura" title="Osamu Shimomura">Osamu Shimomura</a> also won the chemistry prize in 2008. <a href="/wiki/Isamu_Akasaki" title="Isamu Akasaki">Isamu Akasaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hiroshi_Amano" title="Hiroshi Amano">Hiroshi Amano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shuji_Nakamura" title="Shuji Nakamura">Shuji Nakamura</a>, who is an American citizen when awarded, shared the physics prize in 2014 and the Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine was awarded to <a href="/wiki/Yoshinori_Ohsumi" title="Yoshinori Ohsumi">Yoshinori Ohsumi</a> in 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">Infrastructure</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_Japan" title="Transport in Japan">Transport in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Japan_Airlines,_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_(19455285040).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3296" data-file-width="4944" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Japan_Airlines%2C_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_%2819455285040%29.jpg/220px-Japan_Airlines%2C_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_%2819455285040%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Japan_Airlines%2C_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_%2819455285040%29.jpg/330px-Japan_Airlines%2C_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_%2819455285040%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Japan_Airlines%2C_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_%2819455285040%29.jpg/440px-Japan_Airlines%2C_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_%2819455285040%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Japan_Airlines,_Boeing_787-9_JA861J_NRT_(19455285040).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Japan_Airlines" title="Japan Airlines">Japan Airlines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flag_carrier" title="Flag carrier">flag carrier</a> of Japan</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Series_L0.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1875" data-file-width="3333" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Series_L0.JPG/220px-Series_L0.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Series_L0.JPG/330px-Series_L0.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Series_L0.JPG/440px-Series_L0.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Series_L0.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A JR Central <a href="/wiki/L0_Series" title="L0 Series">L0 Series</a> <a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">shinkansen</a> (bullet train)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan's road spending has been extensive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Its 1.2 million kilometres (0.75 million miles) of paved road are the main means of transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> As of April 2012 Japan has approximately 1,215,000 kilometres (755,000 miles) of roads made up of 1,022,000 kilometres (635,000 miles) of city, town and village roads, 129,000 kilometres (80,000 miles) of prefectural roads, 55,000 kilometres (34,000 miles) of general national highways and 8,050 kilometres (5,000 miles) of national <a href="/wiki/Expressways_of_Japan" title="Expressways of Japan">expressways</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> A single network of high-speed, divided, limited-access <a href="/wiki/Toll_road" title="Toll road">toll roads</a> connects major cities on <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shikoku" title="Shikoku">Shikoku</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyushu" title="Kyushu">Kyushu</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hokkaido" title="Hokkaido">Hokkaido</a> has a separate network, and <a href="/wiki/Okinawa_Island" title="Okinawa Island">Okinawa Island</a> has a highway of this type. A single network of high-speed, divided, limited-access <a href="/wiki/Toll_road" title="Toll road">toll roads</a> connects major cities and is operated by toll-collecting enterprises. New and used cars are inexpensive; car ownership fees and fuel levies are used to promote energy efficiency. However, at just 50 percent of all distance traveled, car usage is the lowest of all G8 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-transtatsjp_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-transtatsjp-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since privatisation in 1987, <a href="/wiki/List_of_railway_companies_in_Japan" title="List of railway companies in Japan">dozens of Japanese railway companies</a> compete in regional and local passenger transportation markets; major companies include seven <a href="/wiki/Japan_Railways_Group" title="Japan Railways Group">JR</a> enterprises, <a href="/wiki/Kintetsu_Railway" title="Kintetsu Railway">Kintetsu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seibu_Railway" title="Seibu Railway">Seibu Railway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Keio_Corporation" title="Keio Corporation">Keio Corporation</a>. Some 250 high-speed <a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">Shinkansen</a> trains connect major cities and Japanese trains are known for their safety and punctuality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> Proposals for a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JR%E2%80%93Maglev" title="JR–Maglev">Maglev</a> route between Tokyo and Osaka are at an advanced stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are 175 airports in Japan;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup> the largest domestic airport, <a href="/wiki/Haneda_Airport" title="Haneda Airport">Haneda Airport</a>, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World%27s_busiest_airports_by_passenger_traffic" title="World's busiest airports by passenger traffic">Asia's second-busiest airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> The largest international gateways are <a href="/wiki/Narita_International_Airport" title="Narita International Airport">Narita International Airport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kansai_International_Airport" title="Kansai International Airport">Kansai International Airport</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%C5%ABbu_Centrair_International_Airport" title="Chūbu Centrair International Airport">Chūbu Centrair International Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nagoya_Port" title="Nagoya Port">Nagoya Port</a> is the country's largest and busiest port, accounting for 10 percent of Japan's trade value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Energy_in_Japan" title="Energy in Japan">Energy in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg/220px-Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg/330px-Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg/440px-Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kashiwazaki_Kariwa-April_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Kashiwazaki-Kariwa_Nuclear_Power_Plant" title="Kashiwazaki-Kariwa Nuclear Power Plant">Kashiwazaki-Kariwa Nuclear Power Plant</a>, a nuclear plant with seven units, the largest single nuclear power station in the world</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 46.1% of energy in Japan was produced from petroleum, 21.3% from coal, 21.4% from natural gas, 4.0% from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power_in_Japan" title="Nuclear power in Japan">nuclear power</a> and 3.3% from <a href="/wiki/Hydropower" title="Hydropower">hydropower</a>. Nuclear power produced 9.2 percent of Japan's electricity, as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, down from 24.9 percent the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> However, by May 2012 all of the country's nuclear power plants had been taken offline because of ongoing public opposition following the <a href="/wiki/Fukushima_Daiichi_nuclear_disaster" title="Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster">Fukushima Daiichi nuclear disaster</a> in March 2011, though government officials continued to try to sway public opinion in favor of returning at least some of Japan's 50 nuclear reactors to service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> As of November 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, two reactors at Sendai are likely to restart in early 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> Japan lacks significant domestic reserves and so has a heavy dependence on <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_oil_imports" title="List of countries by oil imports">imported energy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Japan has therefore aimed to diversify its sources and maintain high levels of energy efficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_supply_and_sanitation">Water supply and sanitation</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Japan" title="Water supply and sanitation in Japan">Water supply and sanitation in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokuyama_Dam.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="2304" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Tokuyama_Dam.jpg/220px-Tokuyama_Dam.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Tokuyama_Dam.jpg/330px-Tokuyama_Dam.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Tokuyama_Dam.jpg/440px-Tokuyama_Dam.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokuyama_Dam.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Tokuyama_Dam" title="Tokuyama Dam">Tokuyama Dam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gifu_Prefecture" title="Gifu Prefecture">Gifu Prefecture</a> is the largest dam in Japan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The government took responsibility for regulating the water and sanitation sector is shared between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Health,_Labour_and_Welfare_(Japan)" title="Ministry of Health, Labour and Welfare (Japan)">Ministry of Health, Labor and Welfare</a> in charge of water supply for domestic use; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Land,_Infrastructure,_Transport_and_Tourism_(Japan)" title="Ministry of Land, Infrastructure, Transport and Tourism (Japan)">Ministry of Land, Infrastructure, Transport and Tourism</a> in charge of water resources development as well as sanitation; the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_the_Environment_(Japan)" title="Ministry of the Environment (Japan)">Ministry of the Environment</a> in charge of ambient water quality and environmental preservation; and the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Internal_Affairs_and_Communications" title="Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications">Ministry of Internal Affairs and Communications</a> in charge of performance benchmarking of utilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup></p>
<p>Access to an <a href="/wiki/Improved_water_source" title="Improved water source">improved water source</a> is universal in Japan. 97% of the population receives piped water supply from public utilities and 3% receive water from their own wells or unregulated small systems, mainly in rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<p>Access to <a href="/wiki/Improved_sanitation" title="Improved sanitation">improved sanitation</a> is also universal, either through sewers or on-site sanitation. All collected waste water is treated at secondary-level treatment plants. All effluents discharged to closed or semi-closed water bodies, such as <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Bay" title="Tokyo Bay">Tokyo Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osaka_Bay" title="Osaka Bay">Osaka Bay</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Lake_Biwa" title="Lake Biwa">Lake Biwa</a>, are further treated to tertiary level. This applies to about 15% of waste water. The effluent quality is remarkably good at 3–10 mg/l of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_Oxygen_Demand" title="Biological Oxygen Demand">BOD</a> for secondary-level treatment, well below the national effluent standard of 20 mg/l.</p>
<p>Water supply and sanitation in Japan is facing some challenges, such as a decreasing population, declining investment, fiscal constraints, ageing facilities, an ageing workforce, a fragmentation of service provision among thousands of municipal utilities, and the vulnerability of parts of the country to droughts that are expected to become more frequent due to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Japan" title="Demographics of Japan">Demographics of Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">Japanese people</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_issues_in_Japan" title="Ethnic issues in Japan">Ethnic issues in Japan</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3598" data-file-width="5999" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG/220px-Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG/330px-Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG/440px-Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="507" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg/220px-Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg/330px-Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg/440px-Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bjs48_02_Ainu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a>, an ethnic minority people from Japan</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan's population is estimated at around 128 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_WPP_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN_WPP-229">[229]</a></sup> with 80% of the population living on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honsh%C5%AB" title="Honshū">Honshū</a>. Japanese society is <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistically</a>, ethnically and culturally homogeneous,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MulticulturalJapan_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-MulticulturalJapan-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gentensei_Shinko_Shinbun_2010_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gentensei_Shinko_Shinbun_2010-231">[231]</a></sup> composed of 98.5% ethnic Japanese,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-8"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup> with small populations of foreign workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MulticulturalJapan_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-MulticulturalJapan-230">[230]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Koreans_in_Japan" title="Koreans in Japan">Zainichi Koreans</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Japan" title="Chinese people in Japan">Chinese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Filipinos_in_Japan" title="Filipinos in Japan">Filipinos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazilian_people" title="Brazilian people">Brazilians</a> mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Brazilian" title="Japanese Brazilian">of Japanese descent</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nikkeijin_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-nikkeijin-233">[233]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peruvian_people" title="Peruvian people">Peruvians</a> mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Peruvian" title="Japanese Peruvian">of Japanese descent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Americans_in_Japan" title="Americans in Japan">Americans</a> are among the small minority groups in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> In 2003, there were about 134,700 non-Latin American Western (not including more than 33,000 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forces_Japan" title="United States Forces Japan">American military personnel</a> and their dependents stationed throughout the country)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> and 345,500 <a href="/wiki/Latin_Americans" title="Latin Americans">Latin American</a> expatriates, 274,700 of whom were <a href="/wiki/Brazilians_in_Japan" title="Brazilians in Japan">Brazilians</a> (said to be primarily Japanese descendants, or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nikkeijin" title="Nikkeijin">nikkeijin</a></i>, along with their spouses),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nikkeijin_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-nikkeijin-233">[233]</a></sup> the largest community of Westerners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most dominant native ethnic group is the <a href="/wiki/Yamato_people" title="Yamato people">Yamato people</a>; primary minority groups include the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_people" title="Ryukyuan people">Ryukyuan peoples</a>, as well as social minority groups like the <i><a href="/wiki/Burakumin" title="Burakumin">burakumin</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> There are persons of mixed ancestry incorporated among the Yamato, such as those from <a href="/wiki/Bonin_Islands" title="Bonin Islands">Ogasawara Archipelago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mccormack1999_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-mccormack1999-239">[239]</a></sup> In 2014, foreign-born non-naturalized workers made up only 1.5% of the total population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> Japan is widely regarded as ethnically homogeneous, and does not compile ethnicity or race statistics for Japanese nationals; sources varies regarding such claim, with at least one analysis describing Japan as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiethnic_society" title="Multiethnic society">multiethnic society</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> while another analysis put the number of Japanese nationals of recent <a href="/wiki/H%C4%81fu" title="Hāfu">foreign descent</a> to be of less than 0.5% of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gentensei_Shinko_Shinbun_2010_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gentensei_Shinko_Shinbun_2010-231">[231]</a></sup> Most Japanese continue to see Japan as a <a href="/wiki/Monoculturalism" title="Monoculturalism">monocultural society</a>. Former Japanese Prime Minister and current Finance Minister <a href="/wiki/Tar%C5%8D_As%C5%8D" title="Tarō Asō">Tarō Asō</a> described Japan as being a nation of "one race, one civilization, one language and one culture", which drew criticism from representatives of ethnic minorities such as the <a href="/wiki/Ainu_people" title="Ainu people">Ainu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan has the second longest overall <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> at birth of any country in the world: 83.5 years for persons born in the period 2010–2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Table_A.17_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Table_A.17-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haaretz.com_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-haaretz.com-243">[243]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Aging_of_Japan" title="Aging of Japan">Japanese population is rapidly aging</a> as a result of a <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93World_War_II_baby_boom" title="Post–World War II baby boom">post–World War II baby boom</a> followed by a decrease in birth rates. In 2012, about 24.1 percent of the population was over 65, and the proportion is projected to rise to almost 40 percent by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-handbook_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-244">[244]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Japan" title="Religion in Japan">Religion in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:200px">

<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:FireBrick; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Folk <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, or "not religious"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[note 1]</a></sup> (51.82%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Yellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Buddhism</a> (34.9%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:GreenYellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Shinto_sects_and_schools#Shint.C5.8D_inspired_religions" title="Shinto sects and schools">Shinto organisations and others</a> (4%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:DodgerBlue; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> (2.3%)</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:Black; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> No answer (6.98%)</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Itsukushima_Gate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2803" data-file-width="4200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/220px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/330px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Itsukushima_Gate.jpg/440px-Itsukushima_Gate.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phoenix_Hall,_Byodo-in,_November_2016_-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2812" data-file-width="5377" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg/220px-Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg/330px-Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg/440px-Phoenix_Hall%2C_Byodo-in%2C_November_2016_-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

The <a href="/wiki/By%C5%8Dd%C5%8D-in" title="Byōdō-in">Byōdō-in</a> Buddhist temple, located in <a href="/wiki/Uji,_Kyoto" title="Uji, Kyoto">Uji, Kyoto</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japan has full religious freedom based on Article 20 of its <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">Constitution</a>. Upper estimates suggest that 84–96 percent of the Japanese population subscribe to <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> as its indigenous religion (50% to 80% of which considering degrees of syncretism with <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shinbutsu-sh%C5%ABg%C5%8D" title="Shinbutsu-shūgō">shinbutsu-shūgō</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReischauerJansen215_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReischauerJansen215-247">[246]</a></sup> However, these estimates are based on people <a href="/wiki/Danka_system" title="Danka system">affiliated</a> with a temple, rather than the number of true believers. The number of <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">Shinto shrines</a> in Japan is estimated to be around 100,000. Other studies have suggested that only 30 percent of the population identify themselves as belonging to a religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kisala_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kisala-248">[247]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Edwin_O._Reischauer" title="Edwin O. Reischauer">Edwin Reischauer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marius_Jansen" title="Marius Jansen">Marius Jansen</a>, some 70–80% of the Japanese do not consider themselves believers in any religion. Nevertheless, the level of participation remains high, especially during <a href="/wiki/Japanese_festivals" title="Japanese festivals">festivals</a> and occasions such as the <a href="/wiki/Hatsum%C5%8Dde" title="Hatsumōde">first shrine visit</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">New Year</a>. <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> from China have also influenced Japanese beliefs and customs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[248]</a></sup> Japanese streets are decorated on <a href="/wiki/Tanabata" title="Tanabata">Tanabata</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obon" title="Obon">Obon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReischauerJansen215_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReischauerJansen215-247">[246]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shinto is the largest religion in Japan, practiced by nearly 80% of the population, yet only a small percentage of these identify themselves as "Shintoists" in surveys. This is due to the fact that "Shinto" has different meanings in Japan: most of the Japanese attend Shinto shrines and beseech kami without belonging to Shinto organisations, and since there are no formal rituals to become a member of folk "Shinto", "Shinto membership" is often estimated counting those who join organised Shinto sects. Shinto has 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">shrines</a> and 78,890 <a href="/wiki/Kannushi" title="Kannushi">priests</a> in the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BY65_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-BY65-250">[249]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> first arrived in Japan in the 6th century; it was introduced in the year 538 or 552<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown.2C_1993._p._455_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown.2C_1993._p._455-251">[250]</a></sup> from the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a> in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown.2C_1993._p._455_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brown.2C_1993._p._455-251">[250]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> was first introduced into Japan by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> missions starting in 1549.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Higashibaba.2C_2002._p._1_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Higashibaba.2C_2002._p._1-252">[251]</a></sup> Today, fewer than 1%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mariko_Kato_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mariko_Kato-253">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mission_Network_News_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mission_Network_News-254">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Heide_Fehrenbach.2C_Uta_G._Poiger_2000_62_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-Heide_Fehrenbach.2C_Uta_G._Poiger_2000_62-255">[254]</a></sup> to 2.3% are <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[note 2]</a></sup> Most of them living in the western part of the country, where the missionaries' activities were greatest during the 16th century. <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki_Prefecture" title="Nagasaki Prefecture">Nagasaki Prefecture</a> has the highest percentage of Christians: about 5.1% in 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup> As of 2007<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there are 32,036 Christian priests and pastors in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BY65_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-BY65-250">[249]</a></sup> Throughout the latest century, some Western customs originally related to Christianity (including <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Japan" title="Marriage in Japan">Western style weddings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valentine%27s_Day" title="Valentine's Day">Valentine's Day</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">Christmas</a>) have become popular as secular customs among many Japanese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[256]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Japan" title="Islam in Japan">Islam in Japan</a> is estimated to constitute, about 80–90%, of foreign born migrants and their children, primarily from <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup> Much of the ethnic Japanese Muslims are those who convert upon marrying immigrant Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup> The Pew Research Center estimated that there were 185,000 Muslims in Japan in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other minority religions include <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Japan" title="Hinduism in Japan">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, and since the mid-19th century numerous <a href="/wiki/Japanese_new_religions" title="Japanese new religions">new religious movements</a> have emerged in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clarke_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clarke-262">[260]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Japan" title="Languages of Japan">Languages of Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese language</a></div>
<p>More than 99 percent of the population speaks Japanese as their first language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_8-9"><a href="#cite_note-cia-8">[8]</a></sup> Japanese is an <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative language</a> distinguished by a system of <a href="/wiki/Honorific_speech_in_Japanese" title="Honorific speech in Japanese">honorifics</a> reflecting the hierarchical nature of Japanese society, with verb forms and particular vocabulary indicating the relative status of speaker and listener. <a href="/wiki/Japanese_writing_system" title="Japanese writing system">Japanese writing</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Kanji" title="Kanji">kanji</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_character" title="Chinese character">Chinese characters</a>) and two sets of <a href="/wiki/Kana" title="Kana">kana</a> (<a href="/wiki/Syllabary" title="Syllabary">syllabaries</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Cursive_script_(East_Asia)" title="Cursive script (East Asia)">cursive script</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radical_(Chinese_characters)" title="Radical (Chinese characters)">radical</a> of kanji), as well as the <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup></p>
<p>Besides Japanese, the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyuan_languages" title="Ryukyuan languages">Ryukyuan languages</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amami_language" title="Amami language">Amami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kunigami_language" title="Kunigami language">Kunigami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_language" title="Okinawan language">Okinawan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miyako_language" title="Miyako language">Miyako</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yaeyama_language" title="Yaeyama language">Yaeyama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yonaguni_language" title="Yonaguni language">Yonaguni</a>), also part of the <a href="/wiki/Japonic_languages" title="Japonic languages">Japonic language family</a>, are spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> chain. Few children learn these languages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup> but in recent years the local governments have sought to increase awareness of the traditional languages. The <a href="/wiki/Okinawan_Japanese" title="Okinawan Japanese">Okinawan Japanese</a> dialect is also spoken in the region. The <a href="/wiki/Ainu_language" title="Ainu language">Ainu language</a>, which has no proven relationship to Japanese or any other language, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moribund_language" title="Moribund language">moribund</a>, with only a few elderly native speakers remaining in Hokkaido.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup> Public and private schools generally require students to take Japanese language classes as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_language_education_in_Japan" title="English language education in Japan">English</a> language courses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ItaGiappone_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-ItaGiappone-267">[265]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems">Problems</span></h3>
<p>The changes in demographic structure have created a number of social issues, particularly a potential decline in workforce population and increase in the cost of social security benefits like the public pension plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup> A growing number of younger Japanese are not marrying or remain childless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ogawa_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ogawa-269">[267]</a></sup> In 2011, Japan's population dropped for a fifth year, falling by 204,000 people to 126.24 million people. This was the greatest decline since at least 1947, when comparable figures were first compiled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[268]</a></sup> This decline was made worse by the <a href="/wiki/2011_T%C5%8Dhoku_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2011 Tōhoku earthquake and tsunami">March 2011 earthquake and tsunami</a>, which killed nearly 16,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[269]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan's population is expected to drop to 95 million by 2050;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-handbook_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-handbook-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[270]</a></sup> demographers and government planners are currently in a heated debate over how to cope with this problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ogawa_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ogawa-269">[267]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">Immigration</a> and birth incentives are sometimes suggested as a solution to provide younger workers to support the nation's aging population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[272]</a></sup> Japan accepts an average flow of 9,500 new Japanese citizens by naturalization (帰化) per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[273]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_High_Commissioner_for_Refugees" title="United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees">UNHCR</a>, in 2012 Japan accepted just 18 refugees for resettlement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[274]</a></sup> while the United States took in 76,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan <a href="/wiki/Suicide_in_Japan" title="Suicide in Japan">suffers from a high suicide rate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-278">[276]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-Times-279">[277]</a></sup> In 2009, the number of suicides exceeded 30,000 for the twelfth straight year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup> Suicide is the leading cause of death for people under 30.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ozawa-desilva_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-ozawa-desilva-281">[279]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Japan" title="Education in Japan">Education in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/220px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/330px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG/440px-Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tokyo_University_Entrance_Exam_Results_6.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Students celebrating after the announcement of the results of the <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Japan#University_entrance" title="Higher education in Japan">entrance examinations</a> to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Primary schools, secondary schools and universities were <a href="/wiki/Education_in_the_Empire_of_Japan" title="Education in the Empire of Japan">introduced</a> in 1872 as a result of the Meiji Restoration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> Since 1947, compulsory education in Japan comprises <a href="/wiki/Elementary_schools_in_Japan" title="Elementary schools in Japan">elementary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_Japan#Middle_school" title="Secondary education in Japan">middle school</a>, which together last for nine years (from age 6 to age 15). Almost all children continue their education at a three-year senior <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education_in_Japan" title="Secondary education in Japan">high school</a>.</p>
<p>Japan's education system played a central part in the country's recovery and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_post-war_economic_miracle" title="Japanese post-war economic miracle">rapid economic growth</a> in the decades following the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. After World War II, the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Law_of_Education" title="Fundamental Law of Education">Fundamental Law of Education</a> and the School Education Law were enacted. The latter law defined the school system that would be in effect for many decades: six years of <a href="/wiki/Primary_education" title="Primary education">elementary school</a>, three years of <a href="/wiki/Middle_school" title="Middle school">junior high school</a>, three years of high school, and two or four years of university. Starting in April 2016, various schools began the academic year with elementary school and junior high school integrated into one nine-year compulsory schooling program, in hopes to mitigate <a href="/wiki/School_bullying" title="School bullying">bullying</a> and <a href="/wiki/Truancy" title="Truancy">truancy</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Education,_Culture,_Sports,_Science_and_Technology" title="Ministry of Education, Culture, Sports, Science and Technology">MEXT</a> plans for this approach to be adopted nationwide in the coming years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup> In Japan, having a strong educational background greatly improves the likelihood of finding a job and earning enough money to support oneself. Highly educated individuals are less affected by unemployment trends as higher levels of educational attainment make an individual more attractive in the workforce. The lifetime earnings also increase with each level of education attained. Furthermore, skills needed in the modern 21st century labor market are becoming more knowledge-based and strong aptitude in science and mathematics are more strong predictors of employment prospects in Japan's highly technological economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-284">[282]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan is one of the top-performing <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries in reading literacy, maths and sciences with the average student scoring 540 and has one of the worlds highest-educated labor forces among OECD countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup> The Japanese populace is well educated and its society highly values education as a platform for social mobility and for gaining employment in the country's competitive high-tech economy. The country's large pool of highly educated and skilled individuals is largely responsible for ushering Japan's post-war <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_post-war_economic_miracle" title="Japanese post-war economic miracle">economic growth</a>. Tertiary-educated adults in Japan, particularly graduates in sciences and engineering benefit economically and socially from their education and skills in the country's high tech economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-284">[282]</a></sup> Spending on education as a proportion of GDP is below the OECD average. Although expenditure per student is comparatively high in Japan, total expenditure relative to GDP remains small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_284-2"><a href="#cite_note-auto-284">[282]</a></sup> In 2015, Japan's public spending on education amounted to just 3.5 percent of its GDP, below the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> average of 4.7%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup> In 2014, the country ranked fourth for the percentage of 25- to 64-year-olds that have attained tertiary education with 48 percent. In addition, bachelor's degrees are held by 59 percent of Japanese aged 25–34, the second most in the OECD after South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OECD_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-OECD-20">[20]</a></sup> As the Japanese economy is largely scientific and technological based, the labor market demands people who have achieved some form of higher education, particularly related to science and engineering in order to gain a competitive edge when searching for employment opportunities. About 75.9 percent of high school graduates attended a university, junior college, trade school, or other <a href="/wiki/Higher_education_in_Japan" title="Higher education in Japan">higher education</a> institution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two top-ranking universities in Japan are the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_University" title="Kyoto University">Kyoto University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[287]</a></sup> Created 16 Nobel Prize laureates. The <a href="/wiki/Programme_for_International_Student_Assessment" title="Programme for International Student Assessment">Programme for International Student Assessment</a> coordinated by the OECD currently ranks the overall knowledge and skills of Japanese 15-year-olds as sixth best in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[288]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Health_in_Japan" title="Health in Japan">Health in Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Health_care_system_in_Japan" title="Health care system in Japan">Health care system in Japan</a></div>
<p>In Japan, health care is provided by national and local governments. Payment for personal medical services is offered through a universal health insurance system that provides relative equality of access, with fees set by a government committee. People without insurance through employers can participate in a national health insurance program administered by local governments. Since 1973, all elderly persons have been covered by government-sponsored insurance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[289]</a></sup> Patients are free to select the physicians or facilities of their choice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[290]</a></sup></p>

<p>Japanese culture has evolved greatly from its origins. Contemporary culture combines influences from Asia, Europe and North America. Traditional Japanese arts include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_handicrafts" title="Japanese handicrafts">crafts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Japanese_pottery_and_porcelain" title="Japanese pottery and porcelain">ceramics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">textiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_lacquerware" title="Japanese lacquerware">lacquerware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sword" title="Japanese sword">swords</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_traditional_dolls" title="Japanese traditional dolls">dolls</a>; performances of <a href="/wiki/Bunraku" title="Bunraku">bunraku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kabuki" title="Kabuki">kabuki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">noh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_traditional_dance" title="Japanese traditional dance">dance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rakugo" title="Rakugo">rakugo</a>; and other practices, the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_tea_ceremony" title="Japanese tea ceremony">tea ceremony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ikebana" title="Ikebana">ikebana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">martial arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_calligraphy" title="Japanese calligraphy">calligraphy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origami" title="Origami">origami</a>, <a href="/wiki/Onsen" title="Onsen">onsen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geisha" title="Geisha">Geisha</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_games" title="List of Japanese games">games</a>. Japan has a developed system for the protection and promotion of both tangible and intangible <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural_Properties_of_Japan" title="Cultural Properties of Japan">Cultural Properties</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Treasures_of_Japan" title="National Treasures of Japan">National Treasures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[291]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites_in_Japan" title="World Heritage Sites in Japan">Nineteen sites</a> have been inscribed on the UNESCO World Heritage List, fifteen of which are of cultural significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unesco1_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-unesco1-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1827" data-file-width="2590" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg/220px-Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg/330px-Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg/440px-Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kinkaku-ji_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kinkaku-ji" title="Kinkaku-ji">Kinkaku-ji</a> or "The Temple of the Golden Pavilion" in <a href="/wiki/Historic_Monuments_of_Ancient_Kyoto_(Kyoto,_Uji_and_Otsu_Cities)" title="Historic Monuments of Ancient Kyoto (Kyoto, Uji and Otsu Cities)">Kyoto</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Special_Places_of_Scenic_Beauty,_Special_Historic_Sites_and_Special_Natural_Monuments" title="List of Special Places of Scenic Beauty, Special Historic Sites and Special Natural Monuments">Special Historic Site, Special Place of Scenic Beauty</a> and a UNESCO World Heritage Site, whose torching by a monk in 1950 is the subject of a <a href="/wiki/The_Temple_of_the_Golden_Pavilion" title="The Temple of the Golden Pavilion">novel</a> by <a href="/wiki/Yukio_Mishima" title="Yukio Mishima">Mishima</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japanese architecture is a combination between local and other influences. It has traditionally been typified by wooden structures, elevated slightly off the ground, with tiled or thatched roofs. Sliding doors (<i><a href="/wiki/Fusuma" title="Fusuma">fusuma</a></i>) were used in place of walls, allowing the internal configuration of a space to be customized for different occasions. People usually sat on cushions or otherwise on the floor, traditionally; chairs and high tables were not widely used until the 20th century. Since the 19th century, however, Japan has incorporated much of Western, <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modern</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-modern_architecture" title="Post-modern architecture">post-modern architecture</a> into construction and design, and is today a leader in cutting-edge architectural design and technology.</p>
<p>The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Japan" title="Buddhism in Japan">Buddhism</a> during the sixth century was a catalyst for large-scale <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_temples_in_Japan" title="Buddhist temples in Japan">temple</a> building using complicated techniques in wood. Influence from the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sui_Dynasty" title="Sui Dynasty">Sui</a> Dynasties led to the foundation of the first permanent capital in <a href="/wiki/Nara,_Nara" title="Nara, Nara">Nara</a>. Its checkerboard street layout used the Chinese capital of <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a> as a template for its design. A gradual increase in the size of buildings led to standard units of measurement as well as refinements in layout and garden design. The introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_tea_ceremony" title="Japanese tea ceremony">tea ceremony</a> emphasised simplicity and modest design as a counterpoint to the excesses of the aristocracy.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Meiji_Restoration" title="Meiji Restoration">Meiji Restoration</a> of 1868 the history of Japanese architecture was radically changed by two important events. The first was the <a href="/wiki/Shinbutsu_bunri" title="Shinbutsu bunri">Kami and Buddhas Separation Act</a> of 1868, which formally separated Buddhism from <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_temples_in_Japan" title="Buddhist temples in Japan">Buddhist temples</a> from <a href="/wiki/Shinto_shrine" title="Shinto shrine">Shinto shrines</a>, breaking an association between the two which had lasted well over a thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[292]</a></sup></p>
<p>Second, it was then that Japan underwent a period of intense <a href="/wiki/Westernization" title="Westernization">Westernization</a> in order to compete with other developed countries. Initially architects and styles from abroad were imported to Japan but gradually the country taught its own architects and began to express its own style. Architects returning from study with western architects introduced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_style_(architecture)" title="International style (architecture)">International Style</a> of modernism into Japan. However, it was not until after the Second World War that Japanese architects made an impression on the international scene, firstly with the work of architects like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kenzo_Tange" title="Kenzo Tange">Kenzo Tange</a> and then with theoretical movements like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolist_Movement" title="Metabolist Movement">Metabolism</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_art" title="Japanese art">Japanese art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_garden" title="Japanese garden">Japanese garden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aesthetics" title="Japanese aesthetics">Japanese aesthetics</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ise_Grand_Shrine" title="Ise Grand Shrine">Shrines of Ise</a> have been celebrated as the prototype of Japanese architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[293]</a></sup> Largely of wood, <a href="/wiki/Minka" title="Minka">traditional housing</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Buddhist_architecture" title="Japanese Buddhist architecture">temple buildings</a> see the use of <a href="/wiki/Tatami" title="Tatami">tatami</a> mats and <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dji" title="Shōji">sliding doors</a> that break down the distinction between rooms and indoor and outdoor space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[294]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_sculpture" title="Japanese sculpture">Japanese sculpture</a>, largely of wood, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_painting" title="Japanese painting">Japanese painting</a> are among the oldest of the Japanese arts, with early figurative paintings dating back to at least 300 BC. The history of Japanese painting exhibits synthesis and competition between native <a href="/wiki/Japanese_aesthetics" title="Japanese aesthetics">Japanese aesthetics</a> and adaptation of imported ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-297">[295]</a></sup></p>
<p>The interaction between Japanese and European art has been significant: for example <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> prints, which began to be exported in the 19th century in the movement known as <a href="/wiki/Japonism" title="Japonism">Japonism</a>, had a significant influence on the development of modern art in the West, most notably on <a href="/wiki/Post-Impressionism" title="Post-Impressionism">post-Impressionism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_297-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-297">[295]</a></sup> Famous ukiyo-e artists include <a href="/wiki/Hokusai" title="Hokusai">Hokusai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hiroshige" title="Hiroshige">Hiroshige</a>. Hokusai coined the term <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a>. Japanese comics now known as manga developed in the 20th century and have become popular worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup> Japanese animation is called <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>. Japanese-made <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game consoles</a> have been popular since the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup></p>
