<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animation">Animation</span></h3>
<p>Japanese animated films, such as <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>, which was largely influenced by Japanese <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> and originally made up of animated <a href="/wiki/Cartoon" title="Cartoon">cartoon</a> works, are popular. Japan is a world-renowned <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Powerhouse" title="Powerhouse">powerhouse</a> of animation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan" title="Music of Japan">Music of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KotoPlayer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="195" data-file-width="300" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/KotoPlayer.jpg/220px-KotoPlayer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/KotoPlayer.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KotoPlayer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Masayo Ishigure playing 13-strings <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">Koto</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japanese music is eclectic and diverse. Many <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Japanese_musical_instruments" title="Traditional Japanese musical instruments">instruments</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">koto</a>, were introduced in the 9th and 10th centuries. The accompanied <a href="/wiki/Recitative" title="Recitative">recitative</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">Noh</a> drama dates from the 14th century and the popular <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan#Folk_music" title="Music of Japan">folk music</a>, with the guitar-like <a href="/wiki/Shamisen" title="Shamisen">shamisen</a>, from the sixteenth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[299]</a></sup> Western classical music, introduced in the late 19th century, now forms an integral part of Japanese culture. The imperial court ensemble <a href="/wiki/Gagaku" title="Gagaku">Gagaku</a> has influenced the work of some <a href="/wiki/20th-century_classical_music" title="20th-century classical music">modern</a> Western composers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[300]</a></sup></p>
<p>Notable classical composers from Japan include <a href="/wiki/Toru_Takemitsu" title="Toru Takemitsu">Toru Takemitsu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rentar%C5%8D_Taki" title="Rentarō Taki">Rentarō Taki</a>. Popular music in post-war Japan has been heavily influenced by American and European trends, which has led to the evolution of <a href="/wiki/J-pop" title="J-pop">J-pop</a>, or Japanese popular music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">Karaoke</a> is the most widely practiced cultural activity in Japan. A 1993 survey by the <a href="/wiki/Agency_for_Cultural_Affairs" title="Agency for Cultural Affairs">Cultural Affairs Agency</a> found that more Japanese had sung karaoke that year than had participated in traditional pursuits such as flower arranging (ikebana) or tea ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[302]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_poetry" title="Japanese poetry">Japanese poetry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="243" data-file-width="517" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/220px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/330px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/440px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
12th-century <a href="/wiki/Genji_Monogatari_Emaki" title="Genji Monogatari Emaki">illustrated handscroll</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Treasures_of_Japan" title="National Treasures of Japan">National Treasure</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The earliest works of Japanese literature include the <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nihon_Shoki" title="Nihon Shoki">Nihon Shoki</a></i> chronicles and the <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB" title="Man'yōshū">Man'yōshū</a></i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_poetry_anthologies" title="List of Japanese poetry anthologies">poetry anthology</a>, all from the 8th century and written in Chinese characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[303]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[304]</a></sup> In the early Heian period, the system of <a href="/wiki/Phonogram_(linguistics)" title="Phonogram (linguistics)">phonograms</a> known as <i>kana</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">Hiragana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">Katakana</a>) was developed. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Bamboo_Cutter" title="The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter">The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter</a></i> is considered the oldest Japanese narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ispmsu_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-ispmsu-307">[305]</a></sup> An account of Heian court life is given in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pillow_Book" title="The Pillow Book">The Pillow Book</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sei_Sh%C5%8Dnagon" title="Sei Shōnagon">Sei Shōnagon</a>, while <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Murasaki_Shikibu" title="Murasaki Shikibu">Murasaki Shikibu</a> is often described as the world's first novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[306]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[307]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Edo period, the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dnin" title="Chōnin">chōnin</a> ("townspeople") overtook the samurai aristocracy as producers and consumers of literature. The popularity of the works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saikaku" title="Saikaku">Saikaku</a>, for example, reveals this change in readership and authorship, while <a href="/wiki/Matsuo_Bash%C5%8D" title="Matsuo Bashō">Bashō</a> revivified the poetic tradition of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kokinsh%C5%AB" title="Kokinshū">Kokinshū</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Haikai" title="Haikai">haikai</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haiku" title="Haiku">haiku</a>) and wrote the poetic travelogue <i><a href="/wiki/Oku_no_Hosomichi" title="Oku no Hosomichi">Oku no Hosomichi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[308]</a></sup> The Meiji era saw the decline of traditional literary forms as Japanese literature integrated Western influences. <a href="/wiki/Natsume_S%C5%8Dseki" title="Natsume Sōseki">Natsume Sōseki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mori_%C5%8Cgai" title="Mori Ōgai">Mori Ōgai</a> were the first "modern" novelists of Japan, followed by <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABnosuke_Akutagawa" title="Ryūnosuke Akutagawa">Ryūnosuke Akutagawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jun%27ichir%C5%8D_Tanizaki" title="Jun'ichirō Tanizaki">Jun'ichirō Tanizaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yukio_Mishima" title="Yukio Mishima">Yukio Mishima</a> and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami">Haruki Murakami</a>. Japan has two <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize-winning</a> authors—<a href="/wiki/Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata">Yasunari Kawabata</a> (1968) and <a href="/wiki/Kenzabur%C5%8D_%C5%8Ce" title="Kenzaburō Ōe">Kenzaburō Ōe</a> (1994).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ispmsu_307-1"><a href="#cite_note-ispmsu-307">[305]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_philosophy" title="Japanese philosophy">Japanese philosophy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="675" data-file-width="480" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg/220px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg/330px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg/440px-Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kitaro_Nishidain_in_Feb._1943.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kitaro_Nishida" title="Kitaro Nishida">Kitaro Nishida</a>, one of the most notable Japanese philosophers</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japanese Philosophy has historically been a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Information_fusion" title="Information fusion">fusion</a> of both foreign; particularly <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western</a>, and uniquely Japanese elements. In its literary forms, Japanese philosophy began about fourteen centuries ago.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Archaeological_record" title="Archaeological record">Archaeological evidence</a> and early historical accounts suggest that Japan was originally an <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic culture</a>, which viewed the world as infused with <i>kami</i> (神) or sacred presence as taught by <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, though it is not a philosophy as such, but has greatly influenced all other philosophies in their Japanese interpretations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[309]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> entered Japan from China around the 5th century A.D., as did <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-312">[310]</a></sup> Confucian ideals are still evident today in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_society" title="Japanese society">Japanese concept of society</a> and the self, and in the organization of the <a href="/wiki/Government" title="Government">government</a> and the structure of <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_312-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-312">[310]</a></sup> Buddhism has profoundly impacted Japanese psychology, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[311]</a></sup></p>
<p>Indigenous ideas of <a href="/wiki/Loyalty" title="Loyalty">loyalty</a> and honour have been held since the 16th century. Western philosophy has had its major impact in Japan only since the middle of the 19th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan,_Kyoto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2004" data-file-width="3404" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan%2C_Kyoto.jpg/220px-Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan%2C_Kyoto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan%2C_Kyoto.jpg/330px-Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan%2C_Kyoto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan%2C_Kyoto.jpg/440px-Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan%2C_Kyoto.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Breakfast_at_Tamahan_Ryokan,_Kyoto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Breakfast at a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ryokan_(Japanese_inn)" title="Ryokan (Japanese inn)">ryokan</a></i> or inn</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg/220px-Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg/330px-Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg/440px-Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toshihana_tea_ceremony.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Maiko" title="Maiko">Maiko</a> preparing teacups for tea ceremony</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Japanese cuisine is based on combining <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple foods</a>, typically <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Japanese_noodles" title="Japanese noodles">noodles</a>, with a soup and <i><a href="/wiki/Okazu" title="Okazu">okazu</a></i>—dishes made from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_(food)" title="Fish (food)">fish</a>, vegetable, <a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a> and the like—to add flavor to the staple food. In the early modern era ingredients such as red meats that had previously not been widely used in Japan were introduced. Japanese cuisine is known for its emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_food" title="Seasonal food">seasonality of food</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[312]</a></sup> quality of ingredients and presentation. Japanese cuisine offers a vast array of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_regional_cuisine" title="Japanese regional cuisine">regional specialties</a> that use traditional recipes and local ingredients. The phrase <i>ichijū-sansai</i> <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">一汁三菜</span>, "one soup, three sides")</span> refers to the makeup of a typical meal served, but has roots in classic <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiseki" title="Kaiseki">kaiseki</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Honzen-ry%C5%8Dri" title="Honzen-ryōri">honzen</a></i>, and <i>yūsoku</i> cuisine. The term is also used to describe the first course served in standard <i>kaiseki</i> cuisine nowadays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yomiuri_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-yomiuri-315">[313]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional Japanese sweets are known as <i>wagashi</i>. Ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Red_bean_paste" title="Red bean paste">red bean paste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mochi" title="Mochi">mochi</a> are used. More modern-day tastes includes <a href="/wiki/Green_tea_ice_cream" title="Green tea ice cream">green tea ice cream</a>, a very popular flavor. Almost all manufacturers produce a version of it. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kakigori" title="Kakigori">Kakigori</a> is a shaved ice dessert flavored with syrup or condensed milk. It is usually sold and eaten at summer festivals. Popular Japanese beverages such as <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a>, which is a brewed rice beverage that, typically, contains 15%~17% <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> and is made by multiple <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermentation</a> of rice. Beer has been brewed in Japan since the late 1800s and is produced in many regions by companies including <a href="/wiki/Asahi_Breweries" title="Asahi Breweries">Asahi Breweries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kirin_Company" title="Kirin Company">Kirin Brewery</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sapporo_Brewery" title="Sapporo Brewery">Sapporo Brewery</a> – the oldest brand of beer in Japan. The <a href="/wiki/Michelin_Guide" title="Michelin Guide">Michelin Guide</a> has awarded restaurants in Japan more Michelin stars than the rest of the world combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-michelin20101124_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-michelin20101124-316">[314]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="text-align:center">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Holidays">Holidays</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Japan" title="Public holidays in Japan">Public holidays in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="412" data-file-width="674" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/220px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/330px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/440px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Young ladies celebrate <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a> (成人の日 <i>Seijin no Hi</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Harajuku" title="Harajuku">Harajuku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Officially, Japan has 16 national, government-recognized holidays. Public holidays in Japan are regulated under the Public Holiday Law (国民の祝日に関する法律 <i>Kokumin no Shukujitsu ni Kansuru Hōritsu</i>) of 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[315]</a></sup> Beginning in 2000, Japan implemented the <a href="/wiki/Happy_Monday_System" title="Happy Monday System">Happy Monday System</a>, which moved a number of national holidays to Monday in order to obtain a long weekend. In 2006, the country decided to add <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_Day" title="Shōwa Day">Shōwa Day</a>, a new national holiday, in place of <a href="/wiki/Greenery_Day" title="Greenery Day">Greenery Day</a> on April 29, and to move Greenery Day to May 4. These changes took effect in 2007. In 2014, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Councillors" title="House of Councillors">House of Councillors</a> decided to add Mountain Day <span style="font-weight: normal">(<span lang="ja">山の日</span><span style="display:none">,</span> <i>Yama no Hi</i>)</span> to the Japanese calendar on August 11, after lobbying by the Japanese Alpine Club. It is intended to coincide with the <a href="/wiki/Bon_Festival" title="Bon Festival">Bon Festival</a> vacation time, giving Japanese people an opportunity to appreciate Japan's mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[316]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[317]</a></sup></p>
<p>The national holidays in Japan are <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">New Year's Day</a> on January 1, <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a> on Second Monday of January, <a href="/wiki/National_Foundation_Day" title="National Foundation Day">National Foundation Day</a> on February 11, <a href="/wiki/Vernal_Equinox_Day" title="Vernal Equinox Day">Vernal Equinox Day</a> on March 20 or 21, <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_Day" title="Shōwa Day">Shōwa Day</a> on April 29, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Memorial_Day" title="Constitution Memorial Day">Constitution Memorial Day</a> on May 3, <a href="/wiki/Greenery_Day" title="Greenery Day">Greenery Day</a> on May 4, <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Day_(Japan)" title="Children's Day (Japan)">Children's Day</a> on May 5, <a href="/wiki/Marine_Day" title="Marine Day">Marine Day</a> on Third Monday of July, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Day" title="Mountain Day">Mountain Day</a> on August 11, <a href="/wiki/Respect_for_the_Aged_Day" title="Respect for the Aged Day">Respect for the Aged Day</a> on Third Monday of September, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autumnal_Equinox" title="Autumnal Equinox">Autumnal Equinox</a> on September 23 or 24, <a href="/wiki/Health_and_Sports_Day" title="Health and Sports Day">Health and Sports Day</a> on Second Monday of October, <a href="/wiki/Culture_Day" title="Culture Day">Culture Day</a> on November 3, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_Thanksgiving_Day" title="Labour Thanksgiving Day">Labour Thanksgiving Day</a> on November 23, and <a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_Birthday" title="The Emperor's Birthday">The Emperor's Birthday</a> on December 23.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[318]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Festivals">Festivals</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_festivals" title="Japanese festivals">Japanese festivals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UenoParkHanami.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1198" data-file-width="1764" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/UenoParkHanami.jpg/220px-UenoParkHanami.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/UenoParkHanami.jpg/330px-UenoParkHanami.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/UenoParkHanami.jpg/440px-UenoParkHanami.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UenoParkHanami.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Popular Japanese festival, <a href="/wiki/Hanami" title="Hanami">Hanami</a> celebration at <a href="/wiki/Ueno_Park" title="Ueno Park">Ueno Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are many festivals in Japan, which are called in Japanese as <i>matsuri</i> (祭) which celebrate annually. There are no specific festival days for all of Japan; dates vary from area to area, and even within a specific area, but festival days do tend to cluster around traditional holidays such as <a href="/wiki/Setsubun" title="Setsubun">Setsubun</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obon" title="Obon">Obon</a>. Festivals are often based around one <a href="/wiki/Ceremony" title="Ceremony">event</a>, with food stalls, <a href="/wiki/Entertainment" title="Entertainment">entertainment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">carnival</a> <a href="/wiki/Game" title="Game">games</a> to keep people entertained. Its usually sponsored by a local <a href="/wiki/Shrine" title="Shrine">shrine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Temple" title="Temple">temple</a>, though they can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular" title="Secular">secular</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jnto.go.jp_321-0"><a href="#cite_note-jnto.go.jp-321">[319]</a></sup></p>
<p>Notable festival often feature processions which may include elaborate floats. Preparation for these processions is usually organised at the level of neighborhoods, or <i>machi</i> (町). Prior to these, the local <a href="/wiki/Kami" title="Kami">kami</a> may be ritually installed in <a href="/wiki/Mikoshi" title="Mikoshi">mikoshi</a> and paraded through the streets, such as <a href="/wiki/Gion" title="Gion">Gion</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kyoto" title="Kyoto">Kyoto</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadaka_matsuri" title="Hadaka matsuri">Hadaka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Okayama" title="Okayama">Okayama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jnto.go.jp_321-1"><a href="#cite_note-jnto.go.jp-321">[319]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Japan" title="Sport in Japan">Sport in Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sumo_ceremony.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Sumo_ceremony.jpg/220px-Sumo_ceremony.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Sumo_ceremony.jpg/330px-Sumo_ceremony.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Sumo_ceremony.jpg/440px-Sumo_ceremony.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sumo_ceremony.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">Sumo</a> wrestlers form around the referee during the ring-entering ceremony</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1050" data-file-width="1600" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg/220px-Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg/330px-Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg/440px-Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hanshin_Koshien_Stadium_2007-21.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_High_School_Baseball_Championship" title="National High School Baseball Championship">National High School Baseball Championship</a> at <a href="/wiki/Koshien_Stadium" title="Koshien Stadium">Koshien Stadium</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">sumo</a> is considered Japan's national sport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[320]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">Japanese martial arts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karate" title="Karate">karate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kendo" title="Kendo">kendo</a> are also widely practiced and enjoyed by spectators in the country. After the Meiji Restoration, many Western sports were introduced in Japan and began to spread through the education system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[321]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japan hosted the Summer Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics" title="1964 Summer Olympics">Tokyo in 1964</a> and the Winter Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1972_Winter_Olympics" title="1972 Winter Olympics">Sapporo in 1972</a> and <a href="/wiki/1998_Winter_Olympics" title="1998 Winter Olympics">Nagano in 1998</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[322]</a></sup> Further, the country hosted the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_Basketball_World_Championship" title="2006 Basketball World Championship">2006 Basketball World Championship</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[323]</a></sup> Tokyo will host the <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Summer Olympics</a>, making Tokyo the first Asian city to host the Olympics twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[324]</a></sup> The country gained the hosting rights for the official <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Women%27s_Volleyball_World_Championship" title="Women's Volleyball World Championship">Women's Volleyball World Championship</a> on five occasions (<a href="/wiki/FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="FIVB Volleyball Women's World Championship">1967</a>, <a href="/wiki/FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="FIVB Volleyball Women's World Championship">1998</a>, <a href="/wiki/2006_FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="2006 FIVB Volleyball Women's World Championship">2006</a>, <a href="/wiki/2010_FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="2010 FIVB Volleyball Women's World Championship">2010</a>, <a href="/wiki/2018_FIVB_Volleyball_Women%27s_World_Championship" title="2018 FIVB Volleyball Women's World Championship">2018</a>), more than any other nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[325]</a></sup> Japan is the most successful Asian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rugby_Union" title="Rugby Union">Rugby Union</a> country, winning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asian_Five_Nations" title="Asian Five Nations">Asian Five Nations</a> a record 6 times and winning the newly formed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IRB_Pacific_Nations_Cup" title="IRB Pacific Nations Cup">IRB Pacific Nations Cup</a> in 2011. Japan will host the 2019 IRB <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">Rugby World Cup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[326]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Japan" title="Baseball in Japan">Baseball</a> is currently the most popular spectator sport in the country. Japan's top professional league, now known as <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Professional_Baseball" title="Nippon Professional Baseball">Nippon Professional Baseball</a>, was established in 1936<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[327]</a></sup> and is widely considered to be the highest level of professional baseball in the world outside of the North American <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">Major Leagues</a>. Since the establishment of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J._League" title="J. League">Japan Professional Football League</a> in 1992, association football has also gained a wide following.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[328]</a></sup> Japan was a venue of the <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_Cup_(football)" title="Intercontinental Cup (football)">Intercontinental Cup</a> from 1981 to 2004 and co-hosted the <a href="/wiki/2002_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2002 FIFA World Cup">2002 FIFA World Cup</a> with South Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[329]</a></sup> Japan has one of the most successful football teams in Asia, winning the <a href="/wiki/AFC_Asian_Cup" title="AFC Asian Cup">Asian Cup</a> four times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[330]</a></sup> Also, Japan recently won the <a href="/wiki/2011_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2011 FIFA Women's World Cup">FIFA Women's World Cup</a> in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[331]</a></sup> Golf is also popular in Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[332]</a></sup> as are forms of auto racing like the <a href="/wiki/Super_GT" title="Super GT">Super GT</a> series and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formula_Nippon" title="Formula Nippon">Formula Nippon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[333]</a></sup> The country has produced one <a href="/wiki/National_Basketball_Association" title="National Basketball Association">NBA</a> player, <a href="/wiki/Yuta_Tabuse" title="Yuta Tabuse">Yuta Tabuse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-consulteny_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-consulteny-336">[334]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Japan" title="Media of Japan">Media of Japan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">

<p><a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">Television</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">newspapers</a> take an important role in Japanese mass media, though <a href="/wiki/Radio" title="Radio">radio</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magazine" title="Magazine">magazines</a> also take a part.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhk.or.jp_337-0"><a href="#cite_note-nhk.or.jp-337">[335]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japanesestudies.org.uk_338-0"><a href="#cite_note-japanesestudies.org.uk-338">[336]</a></sup> For a long time, newspapers were regarded as the most influential information medium in Japan, although audience attitudes towards television changed with the emergence of commercial news broadcasting in the mid-1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nhk.or.jp_337-1"><a href="#cite_note-nhk.or.jp-337">[335]</a></sup> Over the last decade, television has clearly come to surpass newspapers as Japan's main information and entertainment medium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressnet.or.jp_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-pressnet.or.jp-339">[337]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are 6 nationwide television networks: <a href="/wiki/NHK" title="NHK">NHK</a> (<a href="/wiki/Public_broadcasting" title="Public broadcasting">public broadcasting</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nippon_Television" title="Nippon Television">Nippon Television</a> (NTV), <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Broadcasting_System" title="Tokyo Broadcasting System">Tokyo Broadcasting System</a> (TBS), <a href="/wiki/Fuji_Network_System" title="Fuji Network System">Fuji Network System</a> (FNS), <a href="/wiki/TV_Asahi" title="TV Asahi">TV Asahi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TV_Tokyo_Network" title="TV Tokyo Network">TV Tokyo Network</a> (TXN).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-japanesestudies.org.uk_338-1"><a href="#cite_note-japanesestudies.org.uk-338">[336]</a></sup> For the most part, television networks were established based on capital investments by existing radio networks. <a href="/wiki/Japanese_variety_show" title="Japanese variety show">Variety shows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_television_drama" title="Japanese television drama">serial dramas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/News" title="News">news</a> constitute a large percentage of Japanese television show. According to the 2015 NHK survey on television viewing in Japan, 79 percent of Japanese watch television every day. The average daily duration of television viewing was three hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[338]</a></sup></p>
<p>Japanese readers have a choice of approximately 120 daily newspapers with a total of 50 million copies of set paper with an average subscription rate of 1.13 newspapers per household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[339]</a></sup> The main newspaper's publishers are <a href="/wiki/Yomiuri_Shimbun" title="Yomiuri Shimbun">Yomiuri Shimbun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asahi_Shimbun" title="Asahi Shimbun">Asahi Shimbun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mainichi_Shimbun" title="Mainichi Shimbun">Mainichi Shimbun</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Nikkei" title="The Nikkei">Nikkei Shimbun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sankei_Shimbun" title="Sankei Shimbun">Sankei Shimbun</a>. According to a survey conducted by the Japanese Newspaper Association in June 1999, 85.4 per cent of men and 75 per cent of women read a newspaper every day. Average daily reading times vary with 27.7 minutes on weekdays and 31.7 minutes on holidays and Sunday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pressnet.or.jp_339-1"><a href="#cite_note-pressnet.or.jp-339">[337]</a></sup></p>

<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Japanese, 43 of the prefectures are called "ken" (県), Kyoto and Osaka are "fu" (府), Hokkaido is a "dō" (道) and Tokyo is a "to" (都). <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-245"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-245">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Routledge Handbook of Japanese Culture and Society</i> (2011) edited by Victoria Bestor, Theodore C. Bestor, and Akiko Yamagata, pp. 66–67 <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-415-43649-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-415-43649-6">978-0-415-43649-6</a>: 無宗教 <i>mushūkyō</i>, "no religion", in Japanese language and mindset identifies those people who do not belong to organised religion. To the Japanese, the term "religion" or "faith" means <a href="/wiki/Organized_religion" title="Organized religion">organized religions</a> on the model of Christianity, that is a religion with specific doctrines and requirement for church membership. So, when asked "what is their religion", most of the Japanese answer that they "do not belong to any religion". According to <a href="/wiki/NHK" title="NHK">NHK</a> studies, those Japanese who identify with <i>mushūkyō</i> and therefore do not belong to any organised religion, actually take part in the folk ritual dimension of <i>Shinto</i>. Ama Toshimaru in <i>Nihonjin wa naze mushukyo na no ka</i> ("Why are the Japanese non-religious?") of 1996, explains that people who do not belong to organised religions but regularly pray and make offerings to ancestors and protective deities at private altars or Shinto shrines will identify themselves as <i>mushukyo</i>. Ama designates "natural religion" what NHK studies define as "folk religion", and other scholars have named "Nipponism" (<i>Nipponkyō</i>) or "common religion".</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-256"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-256">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to the Dentsu survey of 2006: 1% <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestants</a>, 0.8% members of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, and 0.5% members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" title="Orthodox Church">Orthodox Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dentsu2006_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dentsu2006-9">[9]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>
